Rule on Particular Affirmative Conditional with Universal Part
Author proves that if a particular affirmative conditional and a universal part are true, the other part must be particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that if a particular affirmative conditional is true and one of its parts is universal, then the other part is particular, using the correct syllogistic figure (first or third) based on which part is universal.

Core Idea
A universal part in a particular conditional forces the other part to be particular, proven through categorical syllogism.

This passage presents an important rule about particular affirmative conditionals. If such a conditional is true and one of its parts (antecedent or consequent) is universal, then the conditional holds with the other part being particular. The author provides proofs using categorical syllogisms in the first and third figures, depending on which part is universal. Understanding this rule is crucial for correctly inferring particular propositions from universal ones in conditional logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particular affirmative conditional and the universal part
Locate the conditional proposition starting with 'قد يكون إذا' (it may be that if...) and identify which of its two parts (antecedent or consequent) is a universal proposition (e.g., 'كل أ ب' meaning every A is B).
The universal part is either the antecedent or the consequent.
2
Determine whether the universal part is the antecedent or consequent
Check if the universal appears before the 'فـ' (then) or after it. If it is before, it is the antecedent; if after, it is the consequent.
This choice determines which syllogistic figure to use.
3
Select the appropriate syllogistic figure
If the antecedent is universal, use the third figure (middle term is subject in both premises). If the consequent is universal, use the first figure (middle term is subject in one premise and predicate in the other).
The middle term in each case is the universal part itself.
4
Construct the syllogism with the universal part as middle term
For the third figure: take the universal conditional 'Whenever every A is B, then some A is B' (from immediate inference) and the given particular conditional. For the first figure: take the given particular conditional and the universal conditional 'Whenever every C is D, then some C is D'.
The syllogism yields a new conditional with the other part made particular.
5
Derive the conclusion that the other part is particular
From the syllogism, conclude a revised conditional: if the antecedent was universal, the new conditional has a particular antecedent; if the consequent was universal, the new conditional has a particular consequent.
The conclusion preserves the original truth but with a weakened universal part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن المتصلة الموجبة الجزئية إذا صدقت وأحد طرفيها كلي فقد صدقت هي والطرف الآخر جزئي. ثم الكلي منها إما هو المقدم كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وإنه يبرهن بالشكل الثالث؛ والأوسط هو الطرف الكلي فيقال كلما كان كل «أب» فبعض «أب»، وقد يكون إذا كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د» فقد يكون إذا كان بعض «أب» فـ «ج د». وإما هو التالي كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فكل «ج د»، وإنه يبرهن بالشكل الأول؛ والأوسط هو الطرف الكلي فيقال قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فكل «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «ج د» فبعض «ج د» فقد يكون إذا كان «أب» فبعض «ج د».
And among them: that the particular affirmative conditional, if it is true and one of its parts is universal, then the conditional and the other part are particular. Then the universal part is either the antecedent, as in our saying 'It may be that if every A is B, then C is D', and it is proved by the third figure; the middle term is the universal part, so it is said 'Whenever every A is B, then some A is B', and 'It may be that if every A is B, then C is D', therefore 'It may be that if some A is B, then C is D'. Or it is the consequent, as in our saying 'It may be that if A is B, then every C is D', and it is proved by the first figure; the middle term is the universal part, so it is said 'It may be that if A is B, then every C is D', and 'Whenever every C is D, then some C is D', therefore 'It may be that if A is B, then some C is D'.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a rule for particular affirmative conditional propositions (متصل موجبة جزئية). If such a conditional is true and one of its parts (either antecedent or consequent) is universal, then the conditional holds with the other part being particular. The author gives two cases. First case: the antecedent is universal. Example: 'It may be that if every A is B, then C is D'. The proof uses the third figure: we have 'Whenever every A is B, then some A is B' and the given conditional, yielding 'It may be that if some A is B, then C is D' (i.e., the antecedent can be taken as particular). Second case: the consequent is universal. Example: 'It may be that if A is B, then every C is D'. The proof uses the first figure: we have 'It may be that if A is B, then every C is D' and 'Whenever every C is D, then some C is D', yielding 'It may be that if A is B, then some C is D'. The middle term is the universal part in both.

Key Terms
موجبة جزئية
شكل ثالث
قد يكون

Key Takeaway
When a particular affirmative conditional has a universal part, the other part must be particular, and the proof uses either the first or third figure depending on which part is universal.

Implications of Particular Negative Conditionals: Two Proof Shapes
Author proves implications of a particular negative conditional when antecedent is universal or consequent is particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the transformed conditional from a particular negative conditional when the antecedent is universal or the consequent is particular, using the second and third figures of categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A particular negative conditional reveals new quantity relationships when paired with universal categorical truths.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a logical rule: from a particular negative conditional (e.g., 'It may not be that if every A is B, then C is D'), if the antecedent is universal or the consequent is particular, we can derive a new conditional by shifting the quantity of one component. The proof uses categorical syllogisms in the second and third figures, with the universal part as the middle term. This technique is essential for analyzing conditional statements in Islamic arguments, especially in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage gives two cases: (a) the conditional is true and its antecedent is universal; (b) the conditional is true and its consequent is particular. In both, the conditional itself is a particular negative (متصلة سالبة جزئية).
Case (a) example: 'It may not be that if every A is B, then C is D'. Case (b) example: 'It may not be that if A is B, then some C is D'.
2
Translate into syllogistic form
The proof treats the conditional as a premise alongside a universal categorical statement. For case (a), we use 'Whenever every A is B, then some A is B' (a true universal affirmative). For case (b), we use 'Whenever every C is D, then some C is D'.
The middle term is the universal part: for case (a) it is 'every A is B'; for case (b) it is 'some C is D'.
3
Construct the third-figure syllogism for case (a)
Premise 1 (universal affirmative): 'Whenever every A is B, then some A is B'. Premise 2 (particular negative conditional): 'It may not be that if every A is B, then C is D'. Conclusion (particular negative conditional): 'It may not be that if some A is B, then C is D'. The middle term 'every A is B' appears in both premises, linking them in the third figure.
The derived conditional has a particular antecedent, whereas the original had a universal antecedent.
4
Construct the second-figure syllogism for case (b)
Premise 1 (particular negative conditional): 'It may not be that if A is B, then some C is D'. Premise 2 (universal affirmative): 'Whenever every C is D, then some C is D'. Conclusion (particular negative conditional): 'It may not be that if A is B, then every C is D'. The middle term 'some C is D' appears in both premises, linking them in the second figure.
Here the antecedent becomes universal, while the original had a universal antecedent (the antecedent itself is universal in the original conditional of case (b) – careful: the passage says 'صدقت هي ومقدمها كلي', meaning the conditional and its antecedent are universal, so the derived conditional has a universal antecedent as well, but the consequent changes from particular to universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن المتصلة السالبة الجزئية متى صدقت هي ومقدمها كلي كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان كل «أ ب» فـ «ج د» فقد صدقت هي ومقدمها جزئي، وإنه يبرهن بالشكل الثالث؛ والأوسط هو الطرف الكلي فيقال كلما كان كل «أب» فبعض «أب»، وقد لا يكون إذا كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د» فقد لا يكون إذا كان بعض «أب» فـ «ج د». وكذلك إذا صدقت هي وتاليها جزئي كقولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فبعض «ج د» فقد صدقت هي ومقدمها كلي، وإنه يبرهن بالشكل الثاني؛ والأوسط هو الطرف الجزئي فيقال قد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فبعض «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «ج د» فبعض «ج د» فقد لا يكون إذا كان «أب» فكل «ج د».
And among them: that the particular negative conditional, whenever it is true and its antecedent is universal, as in our saying 'It may not be that if every A is B, then C is D', then the conditional and its antecedent are particular, and it is proved by the third figure; the middle term is the universal part, so it is said 'Whenever every A is B, then some A is B', and 'It may not be that if every A is B, then C is D', therefore 'It may not be that if some A is B, then C is D'. And likewise, if the conditional is true and its consequent is particular, as in our saying 'It may not be that if A is B, then some C is D', then the conditional and its antecedent are universal, and it is proved by the second figure; the middle term is the particular part, so it is said 'It may not be that if A is B, then some C is D', and 'Whenever every C is D, then some C is D', therefore 'It may not be that if A is B, then every C is D'.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a rule for particular negative conditional propositions (متصل سالبة جزئية). Two cases are given. First case: the conditional is true with a universal antecedent. Example: 'It may not be that if every A is B, then C is D'. The proof uses the third figure: we have 'Whenever every A is B, then some A is B' and the given conditional, yielding 'It may not be that if some A is B, then C is D' (i.e., the antecedent becomes particular). Second case: the conditional is true with a particular consequent. Example: 'It may not be that if A is B, then some C is D'. The proof uses the second figure: we have 'It may not be that if A is B, then some C is D' and 'Whenever every C is D, then some C is D', yielding 'It may not be that if A is B, then every C is D' (i.e., the antecedent becomes universal). Note: the text says 'صدقت هي ومقدمها كلي' meaning the conditional and its antecedent are universal in the second case, but careful: the conditional itself is particular, so it means the antecedent part becomes universal.

Deriving the Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author enumerates 164 productive moods of the first figure by combining 12 premise-moods with 16 term-variations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of productive moods in the first figure by systematically combining valid premise-moods with term-arrangements.

Core Idea
Productive moods are derived from only 12 premise-moods multiplied by specific term-arrangements.

This passage details how the 164 productive moods of the first figure are generated. Understanding this derivation is essential for mastering syllogistic theory and recognizing valid argument forms used in Islamic kalam and fiqh. We will break down the enumeration into clear steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 12 valid premise-moods
From the 16 possible combinations of quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) for the two premises, only 12 are productive because both premises cannot be particular. The passage lists three groups of four: both universal, minor particular & major universal, minor universal & major particular.
Four are invalid due to two particulars.
2
Understand the 16 term-arrangements from the extremes
The passage introduces 16 moods based on the state of the two shared extremes (the two terms besides the middle). These represent different arrangements of the subject, predicate, and middle in the premises.
3
Multiply the first group of four by the 16 term-arrangements
Each of the first four premise-moods (both universal) when combined with all 16 term-arrangements produces a valid syllogism, yielding 64 moods (4 × 16).
4
Multiply the middle group of four by 12 term-arrangements
Each of the middle four premise-moods (minor particular) when combined with 12 of the 16 term-arrangements produces a valid syllogism, yielding 48 moods (4 × 12).
The passage does not explicitly state the remaining 52 moods; the total 164 suggests additional combinations from the last group.
5
Compute the total productive moods
Add 64 + 48 = 112, but the passage says 164. Therefore, there must be further combinations from the last group of four premise-moods (minor universal, major particular) with term-arrangements not detailed here, or a different interpretation. The total 164 is stated as a result of all conditions.
The passage's arithmetic: 4×16 + 4×12 = 112, but more combinations are implied to reach 164.

أما الشكل الأول فالمنتج منه بحسب الشرائط المذكورة مائة وأربعة وستون ضرباً ضرورة أن المنتج من الضروب الستة عشر الحاصلة باعتبار حال المقدمتين كما مر اثني عشر وهي الضروب التي الحاصلة لا تكون كل واحدة من المقدمتين فيها جزئية؛ إذ لا قياس عن جزئيتين: أربعة من الضروب الاثني عشر ما يكون من موجبتين كليتين، ومن سالبتين كليتين، ومن موجبة كلية وسالبة كلية، ومن سالبة كلية وموجبة كلية . وأربعة منها ما تكون الصغرى فيه جزئية دون الكبرى؛ وذلك من موجبة جزئية وموجبة كلية، ومن موجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية، ومن سالبة جزئية وموجبة كلية، ومن سالبة جزئية وسالبة كلية. وأربعة منها ما تكون الصغرى فيه كلية دون الكبرى؛ وذلك من موجبة كلية وموجبة جزئية، ومن موجبة كلية وسالبة جزئية، ومن سالبة كلية وموجبة جزئية، ومن سالبة كلية وسالبة جزئية. والضروب الستة عشر الحاصلة باعتبار حال الطرفين المتشاركين فهي التي يقع عليها كل واحد من الضروب الاثني عشر. ثمَّ كل واحد من الأربعة الأولى إذا وقع على هيئة الضروب الستة عشر فإنه ينتج ويحصل من ذلك أربعة وستون ضربًا وهي الحاصل من ضرب أربعة في ستة عشر. وكل واحد من الأربعة الوسطى إذا وقع على هيئة كل واحد من اثني عشر ضربًا من تلك الضروب فإنه ينتج. والضروب الاثنا عشر منها ما يكون المقدمان في الصغرى والكبرى من موجبتين كليتين، أو من موجب كلي وسالب كلي، أو من موجب كلي وموجب جزئي، أو من موجب كلي وسالب جزئي، أو موجبتين جزئيتين، أو من موجب جزئي وموجب كلي أو من موجب جزئي وسالب كلي، أو من موجب جزئي وسالب جزئي، أو من سالبتين كليتين، أو من سالب كلي وسالب جزئي، أو سالبتين جزئيتين، أو من سالب جزئي وسالب كلي: ويحصل من هذا ثمانية وأربعون ضربًا وهي الحاصل من ضرب أربعة في اثني عشر.
As for the first figure, what is productive from it according to the mentioned conditions is one hundred and sixty-four moods, necessarily because what is productive from the sixteen moods obtained by considering the state of the two premises, as previously mentioned, are twelve — they are the moods that are such that not each of the two premises in them is particular, since there is no syllogism from two particulars: four of the twelve moods are from two universal affirmatives, from two universal negatives, from a universal affirmative and a universal negative, and from a universal negative and a universal affirmative. And four of them are where the minor premise is particular without the major; that is, from a particular affirmative and a universal affirmative, from a particular affirmative and a universal negative, from a particular negative and a universal affirmative, and from a particular negative and a universal negative. And four of them are where the minor premise is universal without the major; that is, from a universal affirmative and a particular affirmative, from a universal affirmative and a particular negative, from a universal negative and a particular affirmative, and from a universal negative and a particular negative. The sixteen moods obtained by considering the state of the two shared extremes are those upon which each of the twelve moods falls. Then each of the first four, when it falls upon the form of the sixteen moods, produces a result, and from that arise sixty-four moods — the product of multiplying four by sixteen. And each of the middle four, when it falls upon the form of each of twelve of those moods, produces a result. And those twelve moods are where the two premises in the minor and major are from two universal affirmatives, or from a universal affirmative and a universal negative, or from a universal affirmative and a particular affirmative, or from a universal affirmative and a particular negative, or from two particular affirmatives, or from a particular affirmative and a universal affirmative, or from a particular affirmative and a universal negative, or from a particular affirmative and a particular negative, or from two universal negatives, or from a universal negative and a particular negative, or from two particular negatives, or from a particular negative and a universal negative. And from this arise forty-eight moods — the product of multiplying four by twelve.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the productive moods (ضروب منتجة) of the first figure of the syllogism. The author states that there are 164 productive moods in the first figure based on the previously mentioned conditions. He explains the derivation: from the 16 possible moods considering the two premises (quantity and quality), only 12 are productive because no syllogism can have two particular premises. These 12 are divided into three groups of four: (1) both premises universal (4 combinations), (2) minor particular and major universal (4), (3) minor universal and major particular (4). Then, he introduces 16 moods from the perspective of the two extreme terms (طرفين متشاركين) — these likely represent different arrangements of the terms (subject, predicate, middle). Each of the 12 premise-moods can be combined with these 16 term-arrangements. Specifically, the first group of four (both universal) when combined with all 16 yields 64 moods (4×16). The middle group of four (minor particular) when combined with 12 of the 16 (some term-arrangements are invalid?) yields 48 moods (4×12). The total is 64+48=112? But the text says total 164. There might be an additional group (the last four?) that is not detailed here, or a different interpretation. The enumeration continues with a list of 12 moods for the second multiplication, but the total 164 is stated at the beginning. This is a technical breakdown for advanced students to memorize the productive moods of the first figure.

Enumeration of the 164 Productive Moods of Categorical Syllogisms
Author enumerates the three groups of moods totaling 164 productive moods, then discusses necessity and begins the first mood example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of productive moods from given premise types and identify the first mood in the first group.

Core Idea
164 moods result from specific quantified premise pairs, but only necessary ones are used in demonstration.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates every productive mood of categorical syllogisms based on the quantity and quality of premises. He calculates 164 moods, then categorizes them into necessary, accidental, and mixed. Understanding this enumeration equips you to see how logical validity is determined by premise form, a tool essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Recall the four categorical propositions: universal affirmative (موجبة كلية), universal negative (سالبة كلية), particular affirmative (موجبة جزئية), particular negative (سالبة جزئية).
2
Understand the restriction
No syllogism is possible from two particular premises, so the author excludes them.
3
Follow the grouping
The twelve possible premise pairs (excluding two particulars) are divided into three groups of four: both universal, minor particular & major universal, minor universal & major particular.
4
Comprehend the multiplication
Each group of four is combined with a set of figures (16, 12, 13) to yield sub-moods (64, 48, 52), summing to 164.
5
Recognize the classification
Each mood can be necessary (لزوميات), accidental (اتفاقيات), or mixed (مختلطات); the author focuses on necessary ones.

من سالبتين كليتين، أو من سالب كلي وسالب جزئي، أو سالبتين جزئيتين، أو من سالب جزئي وسالب كلي: ويحصل من هذا ثمانية وأربعون ضربًا وهي الحاصل من ضرب أربعة في اثني عشر. وكل واحد من الأربعة الأخيرة إذا وقع هذه الضروب يمكن أن يكون من اللزوميات، ويمكن أن يكون من الاتفاقيات ويمكن أن يكون من المختلطات لكن ما يكون من اللوازم ذكره فذلك من اللزوميات فنتعرض لما هو منها كذلك . ولما مر ذكر هذه الضروب اللازمة معرفتها في هذا القسم؛ وذلك من جملة ما يقتضي التعرض لبيان كل واحد منها فردًا فردًا فاللازم هو التعرُّض لكن التعرُّض للبعض منها إذا كان كافيًا فلا حاجة إلى التعرُّض للكل. وما يكون بالتقديم أولى من ذلك The state of the two premises, as mentioned, is twelve — these are the moods that result when neither of the two premises is particular, for there is no syllogism from two particulars. Four of the twelve moods consist of two universal affirmatives, two universal negatives, a universal affirmative and a universal negative, and a universal negative and a universal affirmative. Four of them have the minor particular without the major: from a particular affirmative and a universal affirmative, from a particular affirmative and a universal negative, from a particular negative and a universal affirmative, and from a particular negative and a universal negative. Four of them have the minor universal without the major: from a universal affirmative and a particular affirmative, from a universal affirmative and a particular negative, from a universal negative and a particular affirmative, and from a universal negative and a particular negative. The sixteen moods that result from considering the state of the two shared terms are those upon which each of the twelve moods falls. Then each one of the first four, when it falls upon the form of the sixteen moods, produces, and from that results sixty-four moods, which is the product of four times sixteen. Each one of the middle four, when it falls upon the form of each one of the twelve moods from those moods, produces. The twelve moods consist of the two premises in the minor and major being two universal affirmatives, or a universal affirmative and a universal negative, or a universal affirmative and a particular affirmative, or a universal affirmative and a particular negative, or two particular affirmatives, or a particular affirmative and a universal affirmative, or a particular affirmative and a universal negative, or a particular affirmative and a particular negative, or two universal negatives, or a universal negative and a particular negative, or two particular negatives, or a particular negative and a universal negative: and from this results forty-eight moods, which is the product of four times twelve. Each one of the last four, when it falls upon the form of each one of the thirteen moods from those moods, produces; and the thirteen moods consist of the two premises in the minor and major being two universal affirmatives, or a universal affirmative and a universal negative, or two particular affirmatives, or a particular affirmative and a universal affirmative, or a particular affirmative and a universal negative, or two universal negatives, or a universal negative and a universal affirmative, or a universal negative and a particular affirmative, or a universal negative and a particular negative, or two particular negatives, or a particular negative and a universal affirmative, or a particular negative and a universal negative, or a particular negative and a particular affirmative: and from this results fifty-two moods, which is the product of four times thirteen. Then the sum of these three numbers is one hundred and sixty-four moods. Then each of these moods can be necessary, can be accidental, and can be mixed; but what is from necessities — its mention is that which is from necessary moods, so we shall present what is of that kind. Since the mention of these moods that must be known in this section has passed; and that is part of what requires presenting each of them individually; the necessary thing is to present them, but presenting some of them if it suffices, then there is no need to present all. What is priority-wise more appropriate than that some — that is from the moods that are in the first of the first four, which is sixteen; for being acquainted with them is a means to being acquainted with the rest of the moods. The first of them is what consists of two universal affirmatives as mentioned, and the first of what falls within this part is that the minor premise is a universal affirmative and the major premise is a particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Whenever every AB is CD, and whenever some AB is H, then OUZ produces; it may be that whenever every AH is CD.'

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the productive moods (ضروب) in categorical syllogisms, specifically focusing on the combinatorial possibilities based on the quantity and quality of premises. The author first recalls that there are twelve moods that result when neither premise is particular (since a syllogism cannot have two particulars). These twelve are then divided into three groups of four: one group where both premises are universal, another where the minor is particular and the major universal, and another where the minor is universal and the major particular. Each of these twelve moods is then considered in terms of the sixteen moods that arise from the state of the two shared terms (الطرفين المتشاركين), likely referring to the figures of the syllogism. The calculation proceeds: the first four moods (where both premises are universal) when combined with the sixteen yield 64 moods (4×16). The middle four (minor particular, major universal) combine with twelve specific moods (listed in the text) to yield 48 moods (4×12). The last four (minor universal, major particular) combine with thirteen moods (listed) to yield 52 moods (4×13). The total is 164 moods. The author then notes that each of these moods can be necessary (لزوميات), accidental (اتفاقيات), or mixed (مختلطات), but the focus will be on necessary ones. Since explaining all 164 individually is lengthy, the author chooses to explain only the first group of sixteen from the first of the first four, as understanding them aids in understanding the rest. Finally, the first mood within this group is introduced: it consists of two universal affirmatives, with the minor universal affirmative and the major particular affirmative. An example is given using letters: 'Whenever every AB is CD, and whenever some AB is H, then OUZ produces; it may be that whenever every AH is CD.' This example seems to illustrate a first-figure mood with a universal minor and particular major, though the notation is garbled and likely represents a conditional syllogism. The passage is highly technical and relies on the reader's familiarity with syllogistic theory.

The Syllogism

Enumerating the Possible Moods of Syllogisms
Enumeration of possible mood combinations and justification for focusing on a subset.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of possible moods given premise combinations and modal distinctions.

Core Idea
13 premise pairs × 4 figures × 3 modalities = 156 (or 164) – the arithmetic reveals the combinatorial scope.

The author begins by listing all possible combinations of premise types for any syllogism, then multiplies by the number of figures and further by modal types. Understanding this enumeration is the first step to mastering the taxonomy of valid syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
List the thirteen premise pairs
Identify the 13 combinations of quantity and quality for the minor and major premises as enumerated by the author.
e.g., two universal affirmatives, universal affirmative + universal negative, etc.
2
Multiply by figures
Multiply the 13 by 4 to get 52 moods for categorical syllogisms.
The author states: 'ويحصل من هذا اثنان وخمسون ضربًا' (This yields fifty-two moods).
3
Account for modality
Recognize that each mood can be with necessary, contingent, or mixed premises, giving 52 × 3 = 156, but the text says 164 – note the discrepancy.
The author mentions three modalities: اللزوميات, الاتفاقيات, المختلطات.
4
Focus on necessary moods
The author decides to only discuss necessary moods and within that, only the first mood of the first figure as sufficient.
This prioritization is based on the idea that understanding one key mood facilitates understanding others.

Commentary
This passage is an advanced syllogistic analysis enumerating all possible combinations of premises in the thirteen moods of the four figures, then focusing on proving the first mood of the first figure. The author begins by listing the thirteen possible premise pairs for any syllogism, covering all combinations of universal/particular and affirmative/negative. Multiplying these thirteen by the four figures gives fifty-two moods, but notes that each mood can be composed of necessary, contingent, or mixed premises, yielding a total of 164 (though the text's arithmetic is unclear: 4×13=52, then perhaps 52×3=156, but it says 164). The author then argues it suffices to explain only some moods, specifically the first mood (two universal affirmatives) of the first figure, because understanding it facilitates understanding the rest. Within this mood, the text examines the case where the minor premise is universal affirmative and the major is particular affirmative, symbolized with placeholder terms (أ, ب, ج, د, و, ز). The conclusion is conditional: 'Whenever every A H is J D, then whenever every A H is W Z.' Two proofs are given: first, a reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) assuming the negation and deriving a contradiction via a third-figure syllogism. Second, an alternative proof using the third figure directly, with the minor premise's implication as the middle term. The author presents two variants of this alternative method. Throughout, the text uses conditional language ('كلما كان...فـ') indicating that these are hypothetical syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). The passage is highly technical and meant for advanced students of logic.

Proving the First Mood via Reductio ad Absurdum
Proof of the first mood with a particular major premise using reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof for the first mood when the major premise is particular affirmative.

Core Idea
Assuming the opposite of the conclusion leads to a contradiction via a third-figure syllogism.

The author provides a proof by reductio (khalaf) for the first mood of the first figure, specifically when the minor is universal affirmative and the major is particular affirmative. By assuming the negation of the conclusion and conjoining it with a true premise, a contradiction is derived, thus proving the original conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood
Minor premise: universal affirmative; Major premise: particular affirmative. Conclusion: 'Whenever every A H is J D, then whenever every A H is W Z.'
The author symbolizes with placeholder letters: أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, و, ز.
2
Assume the negation
The contradictory is: 'It is not absolutely the case that whenever every A H is J D, and whenever every A H is W Z.' This means both premises are true but the conclusion false.
This sets up the reductio assumption.
3
Add a true premise
Conjoin with a true premise: 'Whenever every A H is every A B, then whenever every A H is W Z.'
This premise is taken as true to enable the derivation.
4
Derive intermediate conclusions
From the assumed premises, derive 'Whenever every A H is W Z' and 'Whenever some B H is W Z' via the third figure.
The third figure syllogism: middle term is subject in both premises.
5
Reach contradiction
The derived 'Whenever some B H is W Z' contradicts the earlier negation, proving the original conclusion holds.
The contradiction shows the assumption is false, so the conditional is true.

And each one of the last four [figures?], when it occurs in the form of each one of the thirteen moods of those moods, then it produces. And the thirteen moods: among them are those where the two premises, in the minor and major, are two universal affirmatives, or a universal affirmative and a universal negative, or two particular affirmatives, or a particular affirmative and a universal affirmative, or a particular affirmative and a universal negative, or two universal negatives, or a universal negative and a universal affirmative, or a universal negative and a particular affirmative, or a universal negative and a particular negative, or two particular negatives, or a particular negative and a universal affirmative, or a particular negative and a universal negative, or a particular negative and a particular affirmative. And from this results fifty-two moods, which is the product of multiplying four by thirteen. Then the total of these three numbers is one hundred and sixty-four moods. Then each one of these moods can be from the necessary [premises], and can be from the contingent [premises], and can be from the mixed [premises]. But what is from the necessary, its mention is that it is from the necessary, so we will discuss what is from it in that way. And since the mention of these necessary moods for knowledge in this section has passed, that is among what requires tackling the explanation of each one of them individually. So the necessary is the tackling, but tackling some of them if it suffices, then there is no need to tackle all. And what is by priority more worthy than that part, that is from the moods in the first mood of the first four, and it is sixteen; because examining them is a means to examining the rest of the moods. And the first mood among them is what is from two universal affirmatives as has passed. And the first from what is within this side is that whose minor premise is a universal affirmative and whose major premise is a particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Whenever every A B is J D, and whenever some B H is W Z', then it may produce: if whenever every A H is J D, then whenever every A H is W Z. And there are proofs for it from reductio and other [methods]. As for the method of reductio, we say: this is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying: 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A H is J D, and whenever every A H is W Z'; then we conjoin it with a true premise which is our saying: 'Whenever every A H is every A B, then whenever every A H is W Z', because then it follows: 'Whenever every A H is every A B and every A H', and 'Whenever every A B and every A H is some B H' from the third figure, and 'Whenever some B H is W Z' produces 'Whenever A H is W Z'. So from the true premise with the contradictory, as it is said: 'Whenever every A H is every A B, and whenever every A H is W Z, and it is absolutely not the case that whenever every A H is J D, and whenever A H is W Z', then our saying: 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever every A H is every A B, and whenever every A H is J D'; but it was the case that whenever every A H is every A B, then whenever A H is J D; this is a contradiction. As for other than reductio, that is from among aspects, including that it is said: the minor premise, if it is from the necessary of our saying 'It is not that none of H is A', then this implication is the middle; so we say: 'Whenever it is not that none of H is A, then every A B, and whenever every A H is J D, and whenever it is not that none of H is A, then every A B, and whenever every A H is J D, and whenever it is not that none of H is A, then every A B, then whenever every A H is W Z' produces from the third; as for the minor, because if it is from its necessary, then our saying 'Every A H' is implying it and what it implies, which is J D likewise. As for the major, because if it is from the necessary of that, then it is from the necessary of our saying 'Every A H' according to what has passed. And then it follows that every A H is implying our saying 'Some B H', but rather our saying 'Every W Z'. And among them is that it is said: the minor premise, if it is from the necessary of our saying 'Every A H', then our saying 'Whenever every A H is every A B' is the middle; so we say similarly to what we said, so that they produce from the third; as for the minor, it is evident, and as for the major
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an advanced syllogistic analysis enumerating all possible combinations of premises in the thirteen moods of the four figures, then focusing on proving the first mood of the first figure. The author begins by listing the thirteen possible premise pairs for any syllogism, covering all combinations of universal/particular and affirmative/negative. Multiplying these thirteen by the four figures gives fifty-two moods, but notes that each mood can be composed of necessary, contingent, or mixed premises, yielding a total of 164 (though the text's arithmetic is unclear: 4×13=52, then perhaps 52×3=156, but it says 164). The author then argues it suffices to explain only some moods, specifically the first mood (two universal affirmatives) of the first figure, because understanding it facilitates understanding the rest. Within this mood, the text examines the case where the minor premise is universal affirmative and the major is particular affirmative, symbolized with placeholder terms (أ, ب, ج, د, و, ز). The conclusion is conditional: 'Whenever every A H is J D, then whenever every A H is W Z.' Two proofs are given: first, a reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) assuming the negation and deriving a contradiction via a third-figure syllogism. Second, an alternative proof using the third figure directly, with the minor premise's implication as the middle term. The author presents two variants of this alternative method. Throughout, the text uses conditional language ('كلما كان...فـ') indicating that these are hypothetical syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). The passage is highly technical and meant for advanced students of logic.

Checkpoint
In the reductio proof, what does the author conjoin with the contradictory of the conclusion?
A) A new premise from the first figure
B) A true premise about the implication of the minor premise
C) The opposite of the minor premise
D) The original major premise

Key Takeaway
Reductio is a powerful method to prove a conditional by showing its negation leads to a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio argument for the conditional: 'If every intoxicant is forbidden, then every khamr is forbidden.' Use the fact that khamr is an intoxicant.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Alternative Proofs via the Third Figure
Alternative proof by implication as middle term in third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply an alternative proof method using the third figure to validate a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
The implication itself can serve as the middle term to derive the conclusion via a third-figure syllogism.

After the reductio, the author presents alternative proofs that do not rely on contradiction. These proofs use the third figure (shakl thalith) where the middle term is the subject of both premises. By identifying the appropriate middle term, the conclusion follows directly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target conditional
The conclusion is: If every A H is J D, then every A H is W Z.
This is the same conditional from the previous lesson.
2
Recognize the minor premise's implication
The minor premise 'Whenever every A B is J D' implies something about A and H. Specifically, if 'not none of H is A', then every A B is J D.
The author calls this implication 'الملازمة'.
3
Use the implication as the middle term
Treat the implication 'not none of H is A' as the middle term. Then construct two premises: 'If not none of H is A, then every A B' and 'If not none of H is A, then every A H is J D'.
This allows a third-figure structure.
4
Apply the third figure
From these premises, derive 'Every A B and every A H is some B H' via third figure, and then 'some B H is W Z' from the major premise, leading to the conclusion.
The third figure has the middle term as subject of both premises.
5
Confirm the derivation
The author shows two variants, both relying on the third figure. Understand that the middle term can vary.
The second variant uses 'Whenever every A H is every A B' as the middle term.

D) The term 'W Z'

Key Takeaway
The third figure can be applied to conditional syllogisms by using the implication as the middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the middle term in a qiyas argument: 'Nabidh is intoxicating; every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Determine which figure this is and try to reconstruct it as a third-figure argument if possible.

The Syllogism

Reductio Ad Absurdum for Conditional Propositions
Author explains reductio ad absurdum method for proving a conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum proof for a conditional proposition using categorical syllogisms in the third figure.

Core Idea
Assume the opposite, derive a contradiction, and the conditional is proven.

The passage begins with the reductio method to prove a conditional statement. Instead of directly deriving the conclusion, we assume its negation and, together with a true premise, show that a contradiction results. This method is a powerful tool in logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target conditional
The conclusion to be proven is 'Whenever every A is H, then W Z'. Recognize this as the statement we aim to establish.
The passage uses variables: A, H, W, Z represent terms.
2
Assume the contradictory
Assume the negation of the conclusion. The text expresses the contradictory as consisting of two conditionals: 'Whenever every A is H, then J D' and 'Whenever every A is H, then W Z'. This represents the falsity of the original conditional.
The exact representation is technical; focus on the idea of assuming the opposite.
3
Add a true premise
Combine the contradictory assumption with a known truth: 'Whenever every A is H, then every A is B'. This premise is taken as true.
4
Derive a contradiction via the third figure
From the assumption and the true premise, a third-figure syllogism yields the intermediate conclusion 'some B is H'. Then, using 'Whenever some B is H, then W Z', we derive 'W Z'. This contradicts the part of the assumption that denies 'W Z', thus proving the original conditional.
The third figure is essential; the middle term appears as subject in both premises.

So whenever every A is H, then W Z. And from that come proofs by reductio and other [methods]. As for the method of reductio, we say: this is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Not at all if whenever every A is H, then J D, and whenever every A is H, then W Z'. So we combine it with a true premise, which is our statement 'Whenever every A is H, then every A is B', so whenever every A is H, then W Z, because then it follows that whenever every A is H, then every A is B and every A is H, and whenever every A is B and every A is H, then some B is H from the third figure, and whenever some B is H, then W Z. So it produces 'whenever every A is H, then W Z'. Thus from the true premise together with the contradictory, as it is said: whenever every A is H, then every A is B, and whenever every A is H, then W Z, and not at all if whenever every A is H, then every A is B, and whenever every A is H, then J D; and it was the case that whenever every A is H, then every A is B, so whenever A is H, then J D; this is a contradiction. As for other than reductio, that is in several ways. Among them: it is said that if the minor premise [i.e., the antecedent of the implication] is from the implications of our statement 'No H is A', then this implication is the middle term, so we say: whenever not nothing of H is A, then every A is B, and whenever every A is H, then J D, and whenever not nothing of H is A, then every A is B, and whenever every A is H, then J D, and whenever not nothing of H is A, then every A is B, so whenever every A is H, then W Z; they produce [the conclusion] from the third [figure]. As for the minor [syllogism], because if it is from the implications of that, then our statement 'every A is H' implies it and implies what it implies, namely J D likewise. As for the major, because if it is from the implications of that, then it is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H' due to what has passed. Then it follows that every A is H implies our statement 'some B is H', but rather our statement 'every W Z'. And among them: it is said that if the minor premise is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H', then our statement 'whenever every A is H, then every A is B' is the middle term, so we say similarly to what we said so that they produce from the third [figure]. As for the minor [syllogism], it is obvious. As for the major, because if every A is H, then every A is B and every A is H, and whenever every A is B and every A is H, then some B is H, and whenever some B is H, then W Z, so it follows that whenever every A is H, then W Z. And among them: it is said that if the minor premise implies our statement 'every H is B', then our statement 'whenever every A is B, then every H is B' is the middle term, so we say similarly to what we said. As for the minor, because if that is from its implications, then it is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H'. For it is impossible that that is from its implications and it is not from the implications of this, namely our statement 'every A is H'; and it was the case that its not being from the implications contradicts that, namely our statement 'every H is B' upon the realization of our statement 'every A is H'. And it cannot be contradictory while it implies that on both suppositions of the realization of our statement 'every A is H' and its non-realization. As for the major, it is obvious: if it implies the minor premise, and the minor premise implies what the major premise implies, then it implies likewise. And among them: it is said that if the major premise implies our statement 'every H is B', then our statement 'whenever some B is H, then every H is B' is the middle term, so we say similarly to what we said, because if it implies that, then it is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H', so 'every A is H' implies 'every A is B' due to what passed, and implies 'some B is H' likewise. And among them: it is said that if our statement 'every H is B' is from the implications of the consequent of the minor [premise], namely our statement 'J D', and our statement 'whenever J D, then every H is B' is the middle term, so we say similarly to what we said, because if it is from its implications, then it is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H' due to what passed. And among them: it is said that if our statement 'every H is B' is from the implications of the consequent of the major [premise], then our statement 'whenever W Z, then every H is B' is the middle term, so we say similarly to what we said, because if it is from the implications of that, then it is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H' due to what passed. Then it is possible that the middle term is the implication of the minor premise, for example, as in the first [method] on the supposition that it does not imply what is in the second, and vice versa. And similarly for the third, fourth, and others.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents various methods for proving a conditional proposition (specifically, 'Whenever every A is H, then W Z') within the framework of categorical syllogisms. The author first outlines the method of reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). In this method, we assume the negation of the desired conclusion and combine it with a true premise to derive a contradiction. The true premise used here is 'Whenever every A is H, then every A is B'. From the assumption of the negation together with this premise, a syllogism in the third figure yields 'some B is H', and then from another premise 'Whenever some B is H, then W Z', we obtain the conclusion, but this leads to a contradiction with the original assumption. Thus the original statement is proven. The author then moves to direct methods (ghayr al-khalf), enumerating several ways (wujuh). In each way, the middle term is identified as an implication (luzum) between certain propositions. For example, in the first direct method, the middle term is the implication 'if not nothing of H is A, then every A is B' (i.e., the minor premise implies this conditional). This middle term is used to construct syllogisms in the third figure to derive the conclusion. The author carefully explains the reasoning for each premise of the syllogisms. Subsequent methods vary by which premise (minor or major) provides the implication and which proposition is the consequent. Throughout, the author relies on the third figure of the categorical syllogism to produce the conclusion 'some B is H' or similar intermediate steps. The passage demonstrates the versatility of syllogistic logic in proving conditional statements by converting them into categorical syllogisms.

Key Terms
الخلف
ملازمة
الشكل الثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the reductio method described, what is the true premise added to the contradictory assumption?
A) Whenever every A is H, then J D
B) Whenever every A is H, then every A is B
C) Whenever some B is H, then W Z
D) Whenever every A is B, then every H is B

Key Takeaway
Reductio ad absurdum proves a conditional by showing that its negation leads to a logical contradiction when combined with a true premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio proof for the conditional: 'If the Quran is the word of God, then it is preserved.' Assume the contradictory and use the true premise 'Whenever the Quran is the word of God, then it is miraculous.' Derive the contradiction.

Direct Proof Methods for Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains direct (non-reductio) methods for proving a conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply one direct method for proving a conditional proposition by selecting an appropriate implication as the middle term.

Core Idea
Direct proofs find an implication that links antecedent and consequent through a middle term.

After reductio, the author presents several direct methods that avoid contradiction by using an implication (luzum) as the middle term in a third-figure syllogism. These methods demonstrate the flexibility of syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the target conditional
We want to prove 'Whenever every A is H, then W Z' without contradiction. Keep this goal in mind.
2
Identify the middle term
In the first direct method, the middle term is the implication: 'If not nothing of H is A, then every A is B'. This implication is derived from the minor premise's logical relationship.
The minor premise here refers to the antecedent of the conditional being analyzed.
3
Construct two syllogisms in the third figure
Using the implication as a premise along with other conditionals, two third-figure syllogisms are built. For example: from 'If not nothing of H is A, then every A is B' and 'Whenever every A is H, then J D', we derive an intermediate conclusion. Then from that and another premise, we obtain 'W Z'.
The exact steps are intricate; focus on the role of the middle term.
4
Verify logical flow
Check that each syllogism is valid in the third figure. The middle term (the implication) must appear as the subject in both premises of the third figure.

As for the major, because if every A is H, then every A is B and every A is H, and whenever every A is B and every A is H, then some B is H, and whenever some B is H, then W Z, so it follows that whenever every A is H, then W Z. As for the minor, because if that is from its implications, then it is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H'. For it is impossible that that is from its implications and it is not from the implications of this, namely our statement 'every A is H'; and it was the case that its not being from the implications contradicts that, namely our statement 'every H is B' upon the realization of our statement 'every A is H'. And it cannot be contradictory while it implies that on both suppositions of the realization of our statement 'every A is H' and its non-realization. As for the major, it is obvious: if it implies the minor premise, and the minor premise implies what the major premise implies, then it implies likewise. And among them: it is said that if the major premise implies our statement 'every H is B', then our statement 'whenever some B is H, then every H is B' is the middle term, so we say similarly to what we said, because if it implies that, then it is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H', so 'every A is H' implies 'every A is B' due to what passed, and implies 'some B is H' likewise. And among them: it is said that if our statement 'every H is B' is from the implications of the consequent of the minor [premise], namely our statement 'J D', and our statement 'whenever J D, then every H is B' is the middle term, so we say similarly to what we said, because if it is from its implications, then it is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H' due to what passed. And among them: it is said that if our statement 'every H is B' is from the implications of the consequent of the major [premise], then our statement 'whenever W Z, then every H is B' is the middle term, so we say similarly to what we said, because if it is from the implications of that, then it is from the implications of our statement 'every A is H' due to what passed. Then it is possible that the middle term is the implication of the minor premise, for example, as in the first [method] on the supposition that it does not imply what is in the second, and vice versa. And similarly for the third, fourth, and others.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents various methods for proving a conditional proposition (specifically, 'Whenever every A is H, then W Z') within the framework of categorical syllogisms. The author first outlines the method of reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). In this method, we assume the negation of the desired conclusion and combine it with a true premise to derive a contradiction. The true premise used here is 'Whenever every A is H, then every A is B'. From the assumption of the negation together with this premise, a syllogism in the third figure yields 'some B is H', and then from another premise 'Whenever some B is H, then W Z', we obtain the conclusion, but this leads to a contradiction with the original assumption. Thus the original statement is proven. The author then moves to direct methods (ghayr al-khalf), enumerating several ways (wujuh). In each way, the middle term is identified as an implication (luzum) between certain propositions. For example, in the first direct method, the middle term is the implication 'if not nothing of H is A, then every A is B' (i.e., the minor premise implies this conditional). This middle term is used to construct syllogisms in the third figure to derive the conclusion. The author carefully explains the reasoning for each premise of the syllogisms. Subsequent methods vary by which premise (minor or major) provides the implication and which proposition is the consequent. Throughout, the author relies on the third figure of the categorical syllogism to produce the conclusion 'some B is H' or similar intermediate steps. The passage demonstrates the versatility of syllogistic logic in proving conditional statements by converting them into categorical syllogisms.

Middle Term as Entailment in Third Figure Proofs
Enumeration of ways the middle term relates in third figure proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the four well-known types of proofs for third figure moods by identifying the middle term as an entailment relation between premises.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the middle term can be an entailment relation, not just a simple term.

The third figure of categorical syllogism is tricky because both premises share the middle term as subject. To prove conclusions, logicians developed multiple methods. This passage reveals a sophisticated approach: the middle term itself can be a conditional relation that links the premises. Let's unpack how this works and why it matters for building rigorous proofs in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic third figure structure
Recall that in the third figure, the middle term (e.g., A) is subject of both premises: Every A is B, Every A is G. The conclusion links B and G. Here the author starts with a concrete example: if every A is B and every A is G, and from 'every B' and 'every G' we derive 'some B is H', then the conclusion 'Z' follows. Notice how the middle term A drops out.
The variables A, B, G, H, Z are placeholders for terms.
2
Trace the first scenario: minor premise entails a universal
The author says: if the minor premise (every A is B) entails 'every H is B', then the conditional 'whenever every A is B, every H is B' becomes the middle term. This is not a simple term but a relation between propositions. Use proof by contradiction: if this entailment holds, it must be a consequence of the premise 'every A is G'.
The logic is that the middle term now connects the minor premise to the major's terms.
3
Examine the second scenario: major premise entails a universal
Similarly, if the major premise (every A is G) entails 'every H is B', then 'whenever some B is H, every H is B' becomes the middle term. Again, the entailment is traced back to 'every A is G' via the chain of reasoning.
This reverses the relation compared to the first scenario.
4
Understand the four well-known proof types
The author summarizes that there are four types depending on which premise's entailment is used: (1) major premise's entailment to minor's consequent, (2) major premise's entailment to minor premise, (3) the reverse of (2), (4) minor premise's entailment to major's consequent. Each corresponds to a specific mood of the sixteen possible moods.
These are not exhaustive but cover the standard cases.

Because if every A is B and every A is G, and whenever every B and every G, then some B is H, and whenever some B is H then Z; so it follows that whenever every A is G then Z. Among them is that the minor premise is said, if it necessitates every H is B, then 'whenever every A is B, every H is B' is the middle term, and we say similarly as we said: as for the minor, because if that is among its necessary consequences, then it is among the consequences of our statement 'every A is G'. Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic analysis enumerating various ways to prove conclusions in the third figure of categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating a basic case: if every A is B and every A is G, and whenever every B and every G then some B is H, it follows that whenever every A is G then Z. Then he lists multiple scenarios where the middle term is defined by entailment relations between premises. For example, one scenario: if the minor premise entails 'every H is B', then 'whenever every A is B, every H is B' becomes the middle term. The explanation uses proof by contradiction: if that entailment holds, it must be a consequence of 'every A is G'. Similarly for the major premise. Other scenarios involve the middle term being the entailment of the consequent of the minor or major premise. The author then summarizes that there are four well-known types of proofs for the sixteen moods, depending on how the middle term relates the premises. He notes that an objector might say these only work from the third figure, but he declines to repeat earlier discussion.

Second Mood of the Third Figure: Proof by Contradiction
Proof of the second mood by contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction for the second mood of the third figure and verify its validity.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction salvages a valid conclusion from two particular premises.

When both premises in the third figure are particular affirmative, the conclusion is not immediate. The classical logician proves it by assuming the opposite and deriving a contradiction. This lesson walks through that proof step by step, using the original Arabic variables.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and figure
The second mood has a minor premise 'some A is B' (جزئية موجبة صغرى) and a major premise 'some B is H' (جزئية موجبة كبرى). Both are particular affirmative, and the middle term B is subject in both — this is the third figure.
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises.
2
Recognize the desired conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional statement: 'whenever every A is G then Z' — it is the same as the conclusion of the first mood. The proof shows that this follows from the two particulars.
The conclusion is universal conditional, not particular – achieved indirectly.
3
Set up the proof by contradiction (al-khalf)
Assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion (i.e., 'it is not the case that whenever every A is G then Z') and combine it with the true minor premise to form a new syllogism.
The contradictory of a conditional 'if P then Q' is 'P and not Q'.
4
Derive a contradiction with the major premise
From the assumption and the minor premise, deduce a statement that contradicts the major premise 'some B is H'. Since the major is true, the assumption must be false, so the original conclusion is true.
The passage illustrates this with variables: from 'every A is G' and 'some A is B' we derive something that conflicts with 'some B is H'.
5
Confirm the validity of the mood
Because the contradiction is unavoidable, the original conclusion is necessarily true. Thus the mood is valid even though both premises are particular.
This proof is an expansion of the first mood's method; the author notes that other methods are similar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثاني مقدم الصغرى موجب جزئي ومقدم الكبرى كذلك كما يقال كلما كان بعض «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان بعض «ب هـا فـ «وز» ينتج النتيجة المذكورة في الأول لما مرّ من الخلف وغيره. أما الخلف فذلك بضم النقيض إلى الصغرى الصادقة كما في قولنا كلما كان كل «أه» فبعض (أب) فكلما كان كل «أهـا فـ «وز»، وليس البتة إذا كان كلما كان كل «أه» في «ج د» فكلما كان كل «أه» فـ و ز» فإنه ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان كلما كان كل «أه» فبعض «أ ب فكلما كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، وقد كان كلَّما كان كل «أهـ» فبعض «أب» فكلما كان كل «أهـا فـ (ج) دا، هذا خلف. أما بيان الصغرى في الخلف فظاهر لما أن كل «أه» مستلزما لبعض «أب» كان مستلزما بعض «أب» وكل «أهـ»، وكلَّما كان بعض أب» وكل أهـ) فبعض بها. وأما الغير فذلك مثل ما قيل في الأول. وقد يقال أيضًا مقدم الكبرى وهو قولنا بعض ب هه لما كان
The second mood: minor premise particular affirmative and major premise likewise, as it is said: whenever some A is B then J D, and whenever some B is H then Z, it produces the result mentioned in the first [mood] due to what has passed of proof by contradiction and other [methods]. As for proof by contradiction, that is by joining the contradictory to the true minor, as in our saying: whenever every A is G then some A is B, then whenever every A is G then Z; and it is not at all that if whenever every A is G is J D then whenever every A is G is Z, then it produces: it is not at all that if whenever every A is G is some A B then whenever every A is G is J D, and it was [the case that] whenever every A is G is some A B then whenever every A is G is J D, this is a contradiction. As for the explanation of the minor in the contradiction, it is clear, because every A is G necessitates some A is B, so it necessitates some A is B and every A is G, and whenever some A is B and every A is G then some B is H. Commentary
This passage defines the second mood of the third figure, where both premises are particular affirmative (e.g., 'some A is B' and 'some B is H'). It states that this mood yields the same conclusion as the first mood (which was 'whenever every A is G then Z'). The proof is by contradiction: assume the opposite of the desired conclusion and combine it with the true minor premise. The illustration uses a conditional chain: if whenever every A is G then some A is B, and that leads to 'whenever every A is G is Z', but if we assume the contradictory, we derive a contradictory result, thus proving the original. The explanation of the minor in the contradiction is clear: if every A is G necessitates some A is B, then together they imply some B is H (by the major premise of the original mood). The author notes that other methods are similar to those in the first mood.

Second Mood of Conditional Syllogism: Particular Affirmative Premises
Author defines second mood and proves it by reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second mood of conditional syllogism and verify its validity using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Both premises being particular affirmative still yields a valid conclusion.

In the first mood, both premises were universal affirmatives. Now the author introduces the second mood, where both premises are particular affirmatives. At first glance, this might seem weaker, but through careful proof (including reductio), the author shows that a valid conclusion still follows. This lesson walks through the structure and the proof method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the formal structure
The passage gives two conditionals: 'If some A is B then C is D' (minor) and 'If some B is H then W is Z' (major). Note that both antecedents are particular affirmative (some...).
The conclusion claimed is analogous to the first mood's result, linking the minor antecedent to the major consequent through the middle term B.
2
Understand the reductio method (khalaf)
The author assumes the contradictory of the desired conclusion and conjoins it to the true minor premise. This leads to a contradiction, thereby proving the original conclusion.
The specific steps in the passage use an assumption that 'every A is H' and derive a contradiction with the premises.
3
Trace the contradiction derivation
From the assumption 'every A is H', the author deduces 'some A is B' (from the minor) and then using the major, derives the conclusion. But the assumption also leads to the negation of the conclusion, creating an impossibility.
This shows that the denial of the conclusion is inconsistent with the premises, so the conclusion must hold.
4
Examine the alternative proof
The author also mentions another method by re-expressing the major premise using a middle term (the conditional relation between H and B). This reinforces the validity.
Both methods confirm that the second mood is productive.

Source Text

The second mood: the minor premise is particular affirmative and the major premise likewise, as in saying 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever some B is H, then W is Z' yields the result mentioned in the first due to the aforementioned reductio and other methods. As for reductio, that is by conjoining the contradictory to the true minor as in our saying 'Whenever every A is H, then some A is B, so whenever every A is H, then W is Z', and absolutely not if whenever every A is H in the sense of C is D, then whenever every A is H, then W is Z, then it yields absolutely not if whenever every A is H, then some A is B, so whenever every A is H, then C is D, and it was the case that whenever every A is H, then some A is B, so whenever every A is H, then C is D, this is a contradiction. As for the explanation of the minor in the reductio, it is clear because every A is H entails some A is B, so it entails some A is B and every A is H, and whenever some A is B and every A is H, then some B is H. As for the other method, it is like what was said in the first. It may also be said the major premise, which is our saying 'some B is H', since it is among the concomitants of our saying 'every H is B', so our saying 'whenever every A is H, then every H is B' is the middle term, then we say similar to what we said, namely that whenever every A is H, then every B is H, so whenever every A is H, then C is D, and whenever every A is H, then every B is H, so whenever every A is H, then W is Z. That is because it is not productive unless the major premise is actualized with it, and the aggregate is among the things entailed by the minor premise, and this is evident.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines the second mood of conditional syllogisms, where both premises are particular affirmatives. For example, 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever some B is H, then W is Z' produce the same conclusion as in the first mood. The author then proves validity by two methods: first, by reductio ad absurdum (khalaf), where we assume the contradictory of the conclusion and conjoin it to the true minor premise to derive a contradiction. The explanation shows that from the assumption that every A is H, we derive that some A is B (given the minor), and then using the major premise, we derive the conclusion, leading to a contradiction. Second, by another method where the major premise is re-expressed using a middle term (the conditional relation between A, H, and B). The author emphasizes that the syllogism is productive only when the major premise is actualized together with the entailments of the minor premise.

The Fourth Mood of Conditional Syllogism
Author defines the fourth mood and proves it using reductio and the earlier method of finding an implication middle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the premises of the fourth mood and apply the alternative proof method using an implication middle.

Core Idea
The fourth mood uses a particular negative minor and universal negative major; its validity is shown by reductio or an implication middle.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth mood of conditional syllogism. The author defines its structure and provides two proof methods: reductio ad absurdum and an alternative method using an implication middle. Understanding this mood strengthens your ability to evaluate conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood structure
The minor premise is a particular negative proposition (e.g., 'Whenever some A is not B'), and the major premise is a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'Whenever no B is H'). The conclusion follows from these two premises.
These are conditional propositions, not categorical.
2
Understand the reductio proof
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion, and show that this assumption, combined with the premises, leads to a contradiction. This proves the original conclusion necessary.
The author refers to this method as 'al-khalf'.
3
Apply the alternative method using an implication middle
Find an implication middle, such as 'Some A is H', that serves as a common link. From this middle, derive the minor and major premises, and then combine to reach the conclusion.
The composite 'Some A is H and no B is A' is used as a middle term.
4
Verify the proof steps
Check that the composite of the implication middle and the premises yields the minor and major conclusions, confirming the validity of the mood.
The same method applies as in the third mood.

The fourth mood: the minor premise is particular negative and the major premise is universal negative, as it is said: 'Whenever some A is not B, then (C is D)?' and 'Whenever no B is H, then (W is Z)' produces the mentioned conclusion by what passed of reductio and other means; for the contradictory is that which was mentioned. And the true minor is exactly likewise, so the reductio is from the implications as passed, and as for the other, that is what was mentioned likewise. And it may also be said: if the major premise is among the implications of our saying 'Some A is H', then our saying 'Whenever some A is H, then no B is H' is the middle; so we say: 'Whenever some A is H, then no B is H, so whenever every A is H, then (C is D)', and 'Whenever some A is H, then no B is H, so whenever every A is H, then (W is Z)'. As for the major, it is clear because if it is among its implications, then its implicans, which is (W is Z), is also among its implications.

In the alternative method, the implication 'Some A is H' serves as the middle term, and the proof proceeds by showing that from 'Whenever some A is H, then no B is H' we can derive both conclusions for the case when every A is H. The justification for the minor and major steps follows the same pattern as before.

The Fifth Mood: Minor Universal Negative, Major Particular Negative
Author defines the fifth mood, reverses the previous, and proves by reductio and the middle method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth mood (EIO) and reconstruct its proof by reductio and the middle method.

Core Idea
The fifth mood reverses the fourth and is proved via contradiction or using the major's implication as middle.

In this lesson, we explore the fifth mood of categorical syllogisms, where the minor premise is universal negative and the major is particular negative—the reverse of the previous mood. The author demonstrates two proof strategies: reductio ad absurdum and a method using the implication embedded in the major premise as the middle term. Understanding these proofs solidifies the validity of this mood and demonstrates the power of conditional reasoning within categorical logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Note that the minor premise is universal negative ('No A is B') and the major is particular negative ('Some B is not H'). The conclusion is a particular negative about A and something (likely 'Some A is not D' based on context).
The exact conclusion is not stated in this snippet but follows from earlier moods.
2
Recognize it as the opposite of the fourth mood
The fourth mood had minor particular negative and major universal negative; this one swaps them. The proof strategies will mirror the fourth mood with adjustments.
This structural symmetry is common in Aristotle's system.
3
Understand the first proof by reductio
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion. Combine it with the minor premise to derive a contradiction with the major premise. The same contradictory used in the fourth mood works here.
The 'naqīḍ' (contradictory) is the key tool.
4
Trace the alternative proof using the middle method
The author shows that the major premise ('Some B is not H') implies 'No B is H' under a certain condition (if 'Every A is H' is true). This implication ('No B is H') becomes the middle term. Then we derive the conclusion through a conditional chain: 'Whenever every A is H, then no B is H; and whenever every A is H, then (C is D)' — thus the conclusion follows.
The composite 'Every A is H' and 'No B is H' implies the minor premise, justifying the step.
5
Check the completion
The passage ends while explaining the major premise step, but the pattern is clear: the middle method reduces the mood to a first-figure conditional syllogism, ensuring validity.
This method is part of the 'burhān bil-ghayr' (proof by another).

The Sixth Mood of Conditional Syllogism
Author defines and proves the sixth mood of conditional syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sixth mood of the conditional syllogism (qiyas iqtirani muttasil) and explain how its validity is proven by reduction to the first figure.

Core Idea
Two negative particular conditionals yield a valid conclusion when reduced to categorical form.

In the previous lessons, we explored the first five moods of the conditional syllogism. Now we encounter the sixth, which is unique because both premises are negative particular conditionals. The author demonstrates its validity by reducing it to a categorical syllogism of the first figure. Understanding this mood will deepen your grasp of how conditional arguments are evaluated in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's structure
Recognize that both premises are negative particular conditionals. The minor premise is 'Whenever not some A is B, then C is D' and the major is 'Whenever not some B is H, then W is Z'.
The negative particular form 'ليس بعض' is equivalent to 'Not some' or 'No' in standard logic.
2
Parse the logical variables
The variables (أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, و, ز) represent terms in the conditionals. The antecedent of the minor is 'not some A is B' (negative particular categorical) and the consequent is 'C is D'. Similarly for the major.
3
Understand the proof via reduction to first figure
The author states that the conclusion follows because the universal conditional implies the particular conditional, and then reduces to a first-figure categorical syllogism. This technique transforms the conditional premises into categorical statements that enable a syllogistic derivation.
The first figure requires the middle term to be subject in one premise and predicate in the other. This reduction is detailed in earlier lessons.
4
Recognize alternative proofs
The same conclusion can be proved by contradiction (khalf) or using the third figure. This shows the robustness of the mood and its integration with other syllogistic forms.
The third figure proof uses the inversion of the major premise's consequent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب السادس مقدم الصغرى سالب جزئي ومقدم الكبرى كذلك كما يقال كلَّما كان ليس بعض أب فـ «جد»، وكلَّما كان ليس بعض بها فـ «وز» ينتج النتيجة المذكورة لاستلزام المتّصلة الكلية الجزئية المقدم المتّصلة الكلية المقدم من الشكل الأول ، وقد مرَّ الكلام فيه. وكذلك بالخلف كما مر من قبل، وبغيره كذلك فإن ما يلزمه مقدم الكبرى وهو قولنا لا شيء من ب هـ إذا كان من لوازم قولنا كل «أهـ فقد تحقق من الشكل الثالث ما يتحقق منه المطلوب كما مرَّ، فالنتائج في هذه الستة متحدة وهي التي مرَّ ذكرها في الأول.
The sixth type: the minor premise is a negative particular and the major premise is likewise, as is said: Whenever not some A is B, then C is D, and whenever not some B is H, then W is Z.
