Seventh Mood of Conditional Syllogism: Definition and Proof
Seventh Mood of Conditional Syllogism: Definition and Proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to validate the seventh mood of conditional syllogism by constructing a contradiction proof and identifying the middle term using entailment relations.

Core Idea
A negative particular major combines with an affirmative universal minor through entailment.

This passage defines the seventh mood of conditional syllogism and demonstrates its validity through two methods: proof by contradiction (khalf) and an alternative entailment method (ghayr). You will learn how to derive the conclusion and why the mood holds despite a negative particular major premise. Understanding this mood equips you to handle complex conditional inferences in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
The minor premise is 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D' (affirmative universal). The major premise is 'Whenever not some B is H, then W is Z' (negative particular). The intended conclusion is 'Whenever nothing of A is H, then C is D and then whenever nothing of A is H, then W is Z'. Note the shared antecedent.
2
Understand the contradiction proof
Assume the negation of the conclusion (i.e., it is not the case that the shared antecedent leads to both consequents). Combine this with the true minor premise derived from the original premises to derive a contradiction. This shows the conclusion must be true.
The 'true minor' is 'Whenever nothing of A is H, then every A is B', which is derived from the original minor via logical relations.
3
Follow the alternative entailment method (ghayr)
The author uses an entailment: 'Every A is H' is entailed by 'Not every A is B'. This entailment serves as the middle term. Two conditional syllogisms are then constructed to derive the two parts of the conclusion separately, showing that the conclusion follows necessarily.
This method demonstrates how the middle term (al-awsat) functions in conditional contexts.

الضرب السابع مقدم الصغرى موجب كلي ومقدم الكبرى سالب جزئي كما يقال كلَّما كان كل «أب» فـ (ج) دا ، وكلما كان ليس بها فـ او ز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كلما كان لا شيء من «أهـا فـ (ج د» فكلما كان لا شيء [من] أها فـ او ز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان كلما كان لا شيء من «أهــا فـ (ج) د فكلما كان لا شيء من «أهـ) فوز فيضم إلى الصغرى » ـ الصادقة وهي قولنا كلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فكل «أب» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أها فـ (وز» لا ينتج من الثاني ليس البتة إذا كان كلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فكل «أب» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أهــ فـ «ج د»؛ وقد كان كلما كان لا شيء من أه» فكل (أ ب) كلما كان لا شيء من «أب» فـ «جد»، هذا خلف. وأما [١٩٤ ] صدق الصغرى في الخلف فقد مرَّ بيانه من قبل، وأما الغير فيقال إذا كان قولنا كل «أه من لوازم قولنا ليس كل «أب» فهذه الملازمة هي الأوسط فنقول كلَّما كان ليس كل «أب» فكل «أهـ» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان ليس كل «أب» فكل «أه» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أها فـ «وز»، أما الصغرى لا تكون من اللوازم إلا وأن يكون مقدم الصغرى من لوازم ما ينافيه وهو قولنا لا شيء من «أهـ» . وأما الكبرى فلأن مقدم الصغرى لا يكون متحققاً إلا وأن يتحقق مقدم الكبرى لاستحالة هذا المجموع وهو قولنا كل أب وكل بهـا على تقدير أن لا شيء من «أهـ»؛ وقد كان كل «أه» من لوازم هذا المجموع.
The seventh type: the minor premise is affirmative universal and the major premise is negative particular, as is said: Whenever every A is B, then C is D, and whenever not some B is H, then W is Z. It produces: It may be that whenever nothing of A is H, then C is D, then whenever nothing of A is H, then W is Z; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement: Not at all whenever nothing of A is H, then C is D, then whenever nothing of A is H, then W is Z. So it is conjoined with the true minor, which is our statement: Whenever nothing of A is H, then every A is B, then whenever nothing of A is H, then W is Z. Not at all from the second: Whenever nothing of A is H, then every A is B, then whenever nothing of A is H, then C is D. And it was: Whenever nothing of A is H, then every A is B, then whenever nothing of A is B, then C is D. This is a contradiction. As for the truth of the minor in the contradiction, its clarification has passed. As for the other method, it is said: If our statement 'Every A is H' is among the entailments of our statement 'Not every A is B', then this entailment is the middle. So we say: Whenever not every A is B, then every A is H, then whenever nothing of A is H, then C is D; and whenever not every A is B, then every A is H, then whenever nothing of A is H, then W is Z. As for the minor, it is not among entailments unless the minor premise is among entailments of what contradicts it, which is our statement 'Nothing of A is H'. As for the major, because the minor premise cannot be realized unless the major premise is realized, due to the impossibility of this totality, which is our statement 'Every A is B and every B is H' on the assumption that nothing of A is H. And it was that every A is H is among the entailments of this totality.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the seventh mood of the conditional syllogism. It has an affirmative universal minor premise and a negative particular major premise. The example uses: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D' (minor) and 'Whenever not some B is H, then W is Z' (major). The conclusion is a conditional with a negative universal antecedent and two consequents. The proof is given by contradiction: assume the opposite, derive a contradiction using the true minor (which is derived from the premises). Then another method is presented using an 'other' (ghayr) approach, where the middle term is the entailment between statements. The author shows how to construct a syllogism using that entailment, verifying the validity. This demonstrates the logical rigor of reducing complex conditionals to basic forms.

Key Terms
الضرب السابع (al-darb al-sabi'): The seventh mood of conditional syllogism: minor premise affirmative universal, major premise negative particular. [Note: Standard classification; no significant shift.]
خلف (khalf): A proof by contradiction, assuming the negation of the conclusion and deriving an impossibility. [Note: Stable term in logical proofs.]
الغير (al-ghayr): Another method of proof, distinct from contradiction, using a middle term (al-awsat) derived from entailment relations. [Note: This term may vary; here it refers to an alternative reduction method.]

The Eighth Mood of Conditional Syllogism
Author defines and proves the eighth mood with affirmative particular minor and unchanged major premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eighth mood and apply the proof by contradiction and entailment method to verify its validity.

Core Idea
The eighth mood reduces a particular minor to universals, showing no deficit in validity.

We have already seen the seventh mood with universal premises. The eighth mood introduces an affirmative particular minor premise while keeping the major negative particular. At first glance, this combination might seem irregular, but the author demonstrates that it is valid by using a proof by contradiction and a reduction to the universal case. This lesson walks you through that proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The first example gives minor: 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D' (affirmative particular). Major: 'Whenever not some B is H, then W is Z' (negative particular). Note the quantifier 'some' in the minor and 'not some' (i.e., none) in the major.
The variables A, B, C, D, H, W, Z are placeholders for any logical terms.
2
Understand the proof by contradiction (khalf)
The author says the conclusion is the same as the seventh mood. The proof assumes the contradictory of the conclusion and combines it with the true minor to derive a contradiction. This shows the original conclusion must hold.
The contradictory (naqid) is a key tool; it is the negation of the conclusion.
3
Analyze the 'other' method (ghayr)
The author provides an alternative proof using entailment: 'Not every A is H' entails 'Every A is B'. Using this, he constructs a chain of conditionals to derive the required conclusion.
This method reduces the particular premise to a universal relation, demonstrating the breadth of the mood.
4
Compare with the seventh mood
The eighth mood mirrors the seventh except the minor is particular. The proof shows that this change does not affect validity; the same conclusion follows via the same logical structure.
This illustrates how particular and universal premises can be interchanged in certain moods.

الضرب الثامن مقدم الصغرى موجب جزئي ومقدم الكبرى بحاله كما يقال كلما كان بعض أب» فـ (ج) دا ، وكلَّما كان ليس بعض اب هـا فـ «اوز» ينتج النتيجة المذكورة في السابع لما مرَّ من الخلف فإن النقيض هو الذي مرَّ ذكره، والصغرى الصادقة كذلك. وكذلك الغير بعينه لفظا ومعنى. وقد يقال أيضًا إذا كان قولنا كل أب من لوازم قولنا ليس كل «أه فهذه الملازمة هي الأوسط فنقول بمثل ما قلناه كلَّما كان ليس كل «أه» فكل «أب» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فـ ج د»، وكلما كان ليس كل «أه» فكل «أب» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أهـ فـ و ز » ، أما الصغرى فظاهر ؛ وقد كان قولنا ليس كل «أه» من لوازم قولنا لا شيء من «أه» وإنه مما يستلزم قولنا كل «أب». وأما الكبرى فكذلك فإن بيانها هو البيان الذي مرَّ ذكره من قبل.
The eighth type: the minor premise is affirmative particular and the major premise is as before, as is said: Whenever some A is B, then C is D, and whenever not some B is H, then W is Z. It produces the result mentioned in the seventh due to the passed contradiction, for the contradictory is what was mentioned, and the true minor likewise. And the other method is exactly the same in wording and meaning. And it may also be said: If our statement 'Every A is B' is among the entailments of our statement 'Not every A is H', then this entailment is the middle. So we say like what we said: Whenever not every A is H, then every A is B, then whenever nothing of A is H, then C is D; and whenever not every A is H, then every A is B, then whenever nothing of A is H, then W is Z. As for the minor, it is clear. And our statement 'Not every A is H' is among the entailments of our statement 'Nothing of A is H', and it is that which entails our statement 'Every A is B'. As for the major, similarly, its clarification is the clarification that passed before.

Commentary
This passage defines the eighth mood of the conditional syllogism. It has an affirmative particular minor premise and the major premise remains negative particular (as in the seventh mood). The example: 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D' (minor) and 'Whenever not some B is H, then W is Z' (major). The conclusion is the same as in the seventh mood. Ninth Mode of Conditional Syllogism: Premises and Proofs
Explains ninth mode with universal negative minor and universal affirmative major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ninth mode of conditional syllogism and reconstruct its proof using both reductio and the alternative method with implication as middle term.

Core Idea
The ninth mode uses a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major; its proof reveals how implications can serve as middle terms.

This lesson dives into the ninth mode of conditional syllogism, where the minor premise is universal negative and the major universal affirmative. You will see how the conclusion is derived and understand two proof techniques: reductio and the alternative method using implication as the middle term. Mastering this mode sharpens your ability to handle complex conditional arguments in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Recognize that the minor premise is a universal negative conditional (e.g., 'Whenever nothing of A is B, then C is D'), and the major premise is a universal affirmative conditional (e.g., 'Whenever all B is E, then W is Z').
The variables A, B, C, D, E, W, Z are placeholders for terms from any Islamic science.
2
State the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional: 'If whenever nothing of A is E then C is D, then whenever nothing of A is E then W is Z'.
Note that the antecedent of the conclusion uses 'nothing of A is E', which is a new combination.
3
Follow the reductio proof
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion and show it leads to a contradiction using the true minor premise.
This method is called al-khalf, common in Islamic logic.
4
Understand the alternative method
The implication that 'all A is E' is a consequent of 'not all B is E' is used as the middle term. Apply contraposition: 'nothing of A is E' implies 'all B is E'.
This transforms the syllogism into a form with a clear middle term.
5
Reconstruct the syllogism with the alternative method
Use the implication as a middle to derive the same conclusion, showing the logical chain: from 'nothing of A is E' to 'all B is E' to 'nothing of A is B' to 'C is D' and 'W is Z'.
This alternative proof is more direct and shows the internal logical relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب التاسع مقدم الصغرى سالب كلي ومقدم الكبرى موجب كلي كما يقال كلما كان لا شيء من «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «ب هـ» فـ «و ز» ينتج النتيجة المذكورة وهي قولنا قد يكون إذا كان كلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فـ «ج د» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فـ «و ز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ فبضم الصغرى الصادقة وهي قولنا كلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فكل «ب هـ» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فـ «و ز» ينتج قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان كلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فكل «ب هـ» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فـ «ج د»، وهذا هو الخلف لما صدق قولنا كلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فكل «ب هـ» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فلا شيء من «أه» وكل «ب هـ» فلا شيء من «أب»، وكلما كان لا شيء من «أب» فـ «ج د». وأما الغير فذلك مثل أن يقال إذا كان قولنا كل «أه» من لوازم قولنا ليس كل «ب هـ» فهذه الملازمة هي الأوسط فنقول كلما كان كل «أه» فليس كل «ب هـ» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «أه» فليس كل «ب هـ» فكلما كان لا شيء من «أه» فـ «و ز»، أما الصغرى فلأن قولنا ليس كل «ب هـ» إذا كان من لوازم قولنا كل «أه» كان قولنا كل «ب هـ» من لوازم قولنا لا شيء من «أه»؛ وقد كان نقيض الملزوم من لوازم نقيض اللازم ولما كان قولنا كل «ب هـ» من لوازم قولنا لا شيء من «أه» فكان قولنا لا شيء من «أب» من اللوازم أيضا؛ وقد كان قولنا لا شيء من «أب» من لوازم هذا المجموع وهو قولنا لا شيء من «أه» وكل «ب هـ» بالضرورة. وأما الكبرى فظاهر لما كان قولنا كل «ب هـ» من لوازم قولنا لا شيء من «أه» لما مر.
The ninth mode: the minor premise is universal negative and the major premise is universal affirmative, as in saying: Whenever nothing of A is B, then C is D; and whenever all B is E, then W is Z. It produces the mentioned result, which is our statement: It may be that if whenever nothing of A is E then C is D, then whenever nothing of A is E then W is Z. Otherwise, its contradictory would be true. So by adding the true minor, which is our statement: Whenever nothing of A is E, then all B is E; so whenever nothing of A is E, then W is Z. It produces our statement: It is never the case that whenever nothing of A is E then all B is E [and] then whenever nothing of A is E then C is D. This is the reductio, because our statement 'Whenever nothing of A is E, then all B is E' is true, [and] whenever nothing of A is E then nothing of A is E and all B is E, [so] nothing of A is B, and whenever nothing of A is B then C is D. As for the alternative, it is like saying: If our statement 'all A is E' is a necessary consequent of our statement 'not all B is E', then this implication is the middle term. So we say: Whenever all A is E, then not all B is E; so whenever nothing of A is E, then C is D; and whenever all A is E, then not all B is E; so whenever nothing of A is E, then W is Z. As for the minor, because our statement 'not all B is E' if it is a necessary consequent of our statement 'all A is E', then our statement 'all B is E' is a necessary consequent of our statement 'nothing of A is E'. And since the contradictory of the antecedent is a necessary consequent of the contradictory of the consequent, and since our statement 'all B is E' is a necessary consequent of our statement 'nothing of A is E', then our statement 'nothing of A is B' is also a necessary consequent. And our statement 'nothing of A is B' is a necessary consequent of this composite: our statement 'nothing of A is E' and 'all B is E' necessarily. As for the major, it is obvious because our statement 'all B is E' is a necessary consequent of our statement 'nothing of A is E', as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage explains the ninth mode of conditional syllogism, where the minor premise is universal negative (sālib kullī) and the major premise is universal affirmative (mūjib kullī). The example uses the variables: 'Whenever nothing of A is B, then C is D' (minor) and 'Whenever all B is E, then W is Z' (major). The conclusion is a conditional: 'If whenever nothing of A is E then C is D, then whenever nothing of A is E then W is Z'. The proof is given by two methods. The first is reductio (al-khalf): assuming the contradictory of the conclusion leads to a contradiction using the true minor premise. The second is the alternative method (al-ghayr), where the middle term is the implication that 'all A is E' is a consequent of 'not all B is E'. The syllogism is reformulated with this implication. The commentator then explains the logical relationships: if 'not all B is E' implies 'all A is E', then by contraposition, 'nothing of A is E' implies 'all B is E'. Further, from 'nothing of A is E' and 'all B is E' follows 'nothing of A is B', which then gives the conclusion. This is a detailed treatment of converting implications.

الخلف
الملازمة

Tenth Mode of Conditional Syllogism (Particular Negative Minor)
Author begins explaining the tenth mode with particular negative minor and universal affirmative major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the tenth mode of conditional syllogism and describe its two methods of proof: reductio and the alternative method using implication as a middle term.

Core Idea
The tenth mode turns on a particular negative minor and universal affirmative major.

The author introduces the tenth mode of conditional syllogism, which features a particular negative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise. This passage outlines two ways to prove the conclusion: reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf) and an alternative method that exploits the implication relation between the premises as a middle term. Although the explanation is cut short, the logical structure is clear enough to follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the minor premise (particular negative: 'Whenever not some A is B, then C is D') and the major premise (universal affirmative: 'Whenever all B is E, then W is Z'). The conclusion is referred to as 'the mentioned result'.
The conclusion is not explicitly stated in this excerpt, but from context it likely combines the consequents.
2
Understand the reductio method
The reductio method assumes the contradictory of the conclusion and shows it leads to a contradiction using the true minor. The author says it is similar to previous modes.
The details of the reductio are not fully given; the passage only mentions that the contradictory and the true minor are used.
3
Understand the alternative method
The alternative method uses the implication that the minor premise is a necessary consequent of the major premise. This implication becomes the middle term. The syllogism is recast as: 'Whenever all B is E, then not some A is B' leading to the conclusions about C-D and W-Z.
This transforms the original conditional into a chain of conditionals.
4
Formalize the alternative syllogism
Write the steps: (1) Whenever all B is E, then not some A is B; (2) Whenever not some A is B, then C is D; (3) Therefore, whenever all B is E, then C is D. Similarly for the second conclusion involving W and Z.
The passage also gives a variation: 'Whenever nothing of A is E, then C is D' and 'Whenever nothing of A is E, then W is Z'.
5
Recognize the incomplete explanation
The passage cuts off mid-sentence. The full proof requires the following pages. This highlights the importance of continuity in logical exposition.

Tenth Mood of the Third Figure: Proof by Contradiction and Entailment
Validity proof of the tenth mood using contradiction and entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the validity proof for the tenth mood of the third figure using both reduction to absurdity and the entailment method.

Core Idea
The tenth mood's validity is proven by reducing it to the first figure via entailment (mulazama) relations.

The tenth mood of the third figure combines a particular negative minor premise with a universal affirmative major premise. The author provides two methods to demonstrate its validity: the familiar proof by contradiction (al-khalf) and a more intricate method using entailment (al-ghayr). This lesson unpacks both proofs, clarifying how the middle term is identified through conditional relations. Understanding this mood sharpens your ability to manipulate logical relations in complex syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises of the mood
The minor premise is particular negative ('some A is not B') and the major premise is universal affirmative ('every B is H'). They are presented as conditionals: 'whenever not some A is B, then C is D' and 'whenever every B is H, then W is Z'.
The variables أ,ب,ج,د,هـ,و,ز are placeholders. The mood is defined by quantity and quality, not the specific terms.
2
Understand the proof by contradiction (al-khalf)
The author briefly mentions that the conclusion is proved by assuming its contradictory and deriving a contradiction with the true premises. This is the standard reductio ad absurdum method used in prior moods.
The detailed contradiction is omitted here; the focus is on the entailment method.
3
Trace the entailment method (al-ghayr)
Assume the minor premise is a necessary consequence (lazim) of the major premise. Then the entailment (mulazama) between the major and minor becomes the middle term. The author constructs a new syllogism: from 'every B is H' we infer 'not some A is B', then combine with other relations to derive 'no A is H' leading to the original consequent.
This is essentially a reduction to the first figure using contraposition.
4
Verify the logical consistency of the entailment
The author argues that if 'no A is H' does not entail 'every B is H', then the supposed entailment from the major to the minor would fail. He uses the fact that if 'every B is H' entails 'some A is B', then by contraposition, 'no A is H' entails the negation of 'some A is B' (i.e., 'no A is B'?), but the text is intricate.
Focus on the idea: the entailment chain must be transitive and consistent.

The tenth mood: the minor premise is a particular negative and the major premise is a universal affirmative, as it is said: Whenever it is that not some A is B, then C is D, and whenever it is that every B is H, then W is Z. It produces the mentioned conclusion by contradiction and other methods. As for contradiction, it is as previously stated; because the contradictory that was mentioned and the true minor premise are likewise. As for the other method, it is said: if the minor premise is a necessary consequence of the major premise, then this entailment is the middle. So we say: whenever every B is H, then not some A is B; so whenever no A is H, then A J D; and whenever every B is H, then not some A is B; so whenever no A is H, then A W Z. As for the minor premise, because if it is a consequence of it, then our statement 'no A is H' cannot be realized unless it is entailing our statement 'every B is H'; otherwise its being entailing that upon realization would contradict that entailment which is the middle; and that is impossible. For our statement 'no A is H' does not entail our statement 'every B is H' upon realization unless our statement 'every B is H' entails our statement 'some A is B' upon realization of that, namely our statement 'no A is H' with the description of being entailing our statement 'every B is H' necessarily, because that entailment is realized upon the realization of that with that description. And the realization with that description entails the entailed in it, and the necessary also. As for the major premise, likewise, and this is apparent, and it produces from the third figure as mentioned.

Commentary
This passage discusses the tenth mood of the third figure of categorical syllogisms. The mood has a particular negative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise. The author explains two methods to prove the validity of the conclusion: reduction to absurdity (al-khalf) and a method using entailment (al-ghayr). In the reduction to absurdity, we assume the negation of the conclusion and derive a contradiction with a true premise. The other method involves showing that the minor premise is a necessary consequence (lazim) of the major premise, and then constructing a new syllogism in the first figure. The author provides a detailed logical justification of the entailment relations, using the principle that if a statement is a consequence of another, the contrary of the consequence is a consequence of the contrary of the original. This is essentially the logical principle of contraposition. The proof is complex but shows the validity of this mood. An Islamic example: 'Some intoxicant is not nabidh? Actually, a better example: Some [drink] is not [hallal]? Let's construct a valid third figure mood: Some khamr is mubah? That's invalid. We can use: Some animal is not a horse. Every animal is mortal. Therefore, some mortal is not a horse? That's not the conclusion here. Actually, the conclusion here is 'some A is not D' etc. We need a concrete example: Suppose A = drink, B = intoxicant, C = forbidden, D = unlawful? But the variables are arbitrary. To illustrate, we can say: Some drink is not wine. Every wine is intoxicant. So some intoxicant is not drink? That's not correct. The text's variables are abstract. In commentary, we can explain the logical form without a concrete example, but the rule says to use Islamic examples. Since the passage is about the mood's validity, we can note that similar reasoning is used in legal analogy (qiyas) to establish entailments between rulings.

The Eleventh Mood of the Third Figure: Proof by Contradiction and Entailment
Author proves the eleventh mood via contradiction and entailment, noting redundancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof of the eleventh mood (EIO-3) using contradiction and entailment, and identify its relationship to the tenth mood.

Core Idea
The eleventh mood is proven by the same logical machinery as the tenth

The eleventh mood of the third figure combines a universal negative minor with a particular affirmative major. The author shows its validity through a proof by contradiction and an entailment method, emphasizing that the reasoning is essentially the same as the tenth mood. This lesson unpacks that proof and applies it to Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's structure
The minor premise is 'No A is B' (universal negative), and the major premise is 'Some B is H' (particular affirmative). The middle term is B. The conclusion (implied) is 'Some H is not A' (particular negative). This is the EIO mood (Felapton) of the third figure.
The author labels the universal negative as minor and particular affirmative as major, which is a stylistic choice.
2
Understand the proof by contradiction
Assume the conclusion is false (i.e., every H is A). Combine with the minor premise 'No A is B' to derive 'No H is B' via subalternation or conversion. But the major premise says 'Some B is H', which is contradictory to 'No H is B'. Hence the assumption is false, proving the conclusion.
This mirrors the proof used for the tenth mood.
3
Follow the entailment method
The author also provides an alternative proof: if 'every B is H' is a consequence of 'not every A is H', then we can chain entailments: from 'No A is H' we get 'No A is B' (contrapositive-type) and from 'Some B is H' we get 'W is Z' (some conclusion). This shows the same result via logical relationships.
The notion of 'lazim' (necessary consequence) is central here.
4
Check the equivalence to the tenth mood
The author states that the proof is identical in meaning to the tenth mood ('fa-inna min haythu al-ma'na huwa alladhi marra dhikruhu'). Recognizing this redundancy is important for understanding the systematic nature of moods.
This highlights the efficiency of logical proofs across similar structures.

Source Text

The eleventh mood: the minor premise is a universal negative and the major premise is a particular affirmative, as it is said: Whenever it is that no A is B, then C is D, and whenever it is that some B is H, then W is Z. It produces the mentioned conclusion by what passed of contradiction and other methods; because the contradictory is that same contradictory. And the true minor premise is that which has been mentioned, so there is no need to repeat, and likewise the other method, for in terms of meaning it is what has been mentioned. And it may also be said: if our statement 'every B is H' is a consequence of our statement 'not every A is H', then this entailment is the middle. Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh mood of the third figure, which has a universal negative minor premise and a particular affirmative major premise. The author states that the proof is essentially the same as for the tenth mood, using contradiction and the entailment method. He notes that the true minor premise is as previously mentioned, and the other method is identical in meaning. The author then elaborates a slightly different version of the entailment method: if 'every B is H' is a consequence of 'not every A is H', then we can form a new syllogism. This is essentially contraposition again. The proof shows that the conclusion follows by linking the entailments. In Islamic sciences, such reasoning is used when combining a general ruling with a specific exception. For example: 'No intoxicant is halal. Some drink is intoxicant. Therefore, some drink is not halal.' That is a valid third figure mood (Felapton? Actually, that's a different mood). The eleventh mood is like: 'No A is B. Some B is H. Therefore some H is not A.' The author's abstract treatment shows the validity through logical transformation.

Key Terms
نقيض (naqid): Contradictory proposition, used in proof by contradiction. [Note: Standard in logic; in Kalam it refers to an opposite that cannot both be true.]
لازم (lazim): A necessary consequence of a proposition, used to construct entailments. [Note: Crucial in usul al-fiqh for deriving rulings.]

Evaluating the Twelfth Mood with Two Particular Premises
Author begins description of the twelfth mood with particular premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of a third-figure syllogism with two particular premises by applying the rule that at least one premise must be universal.

Core Idea
Two particular premises in the third figure always yield an invalid syllogism.

In this lesson, we examine the twelfth mood of the third figure, which starts with a particular negative minor and a particular affirmative major. Although the passage is cut off, the given information is enough to assess validity. You will learn to recognize why such a mood fails and how to avoid similar errors in juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The passage explicitly states: the minor premise is particular negative (sālib juz'ī) and the major premise is particular affirmative (mūjab juz'ī). Note the quantity (particular) and quality (negative/affirmative) of each.
The example uses the conditional form but the underlying categorical structure is clear.
2
Check the figure and middle term
For the third figure, the middle term must be the subject of both premises. Here the minor is 'some A is not B', so B is the middle if the major also has B as subject. The major is incomplete but likely has B as subject (e.g., 'some B is C').
Even if the major is different, the rule about particular premises still applies.
3
Apply the rule of validity
A valid categorical syllogism must have at least one universal premise. Since both premises are particular, the syllogism is invalid regardless of the conclusion.
This rule holds for all figures, not just the third.
4
Interpret the incomplete text
The author likely listed this mood to demonstrate its invalidity. The missing conclusion and proof would show why it fails. Consult the complete text for the full argument.
The high OCR risk means you should verify with a reliable edition.

The Twelfth Mode of Conditional Syllogism: Particular Premises and Reduction
Author defines the twelfth mode with a particular negative minor and a particular affirmative major, and mentions reduction to first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the twelfth mode of conditional syllogism, where both premises are particular, and explain how it is reduced to the first figure.

Core Idea
Particular premises in conditional syllogisms are validated by implied universal conditionals.

In the study of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), there are various modes based on the quantity and quality of the premises. The twelfth mode is unique because both the minor and major premises are particular: the minor is particular negative and the major is particular affirmative. How can a valid conclusion be drawn from two particular premises? The author explains that the conclusion follows because the universal conditional implies the particular conditional, using a reduction to the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The minor premise 'Whenever some A is not B, then J is D' is a particular negative conditional (some A is not B leads to J is D). The major premise 'Whenever some B is H, then W is Z' is a particular affirmative conditional (some B is H leads to W is Z).
These are symbolized as: (∃A)(¬B → J) and (∃B)(H → WZ) roughly.
2
Recognize the structure
Both conditionals share a common term B, allowing inference. The conclusion is not explicitly given in the passage, but it follows a pattern like 'Whenever some A is not B, then W is Z' or similar.
The shared middle term is B.
3
Understand the reduction
The author says the conclusion is produced 'because the universal conditional implies the particular conditional from the first figure.' That is, if we had universal versions of these conditionals (i.e., 'Every A is B' etc.), they would fit the first figure and yield a universal conclusion, which then implies the particular conclusion.
This reduction shows that the particular premises are logically dependent on universal conditionals.
4
Note the uniformity
The conclusions of the sixth to twelfth modes are the same as those of the seventh mode, indicating a systematic pattern across the modes.

Source Text

As for the reductio and other methods, they are as before in the preceding mode. Then the conclusions in these six are unified and are those mentioned in the seventh.

This reduction to the first figure is the main reasoning method, while the reductio method works as in the previous mode. The author then remarks that the conclusions of these six modes (likely the seventh through twelfth) are the same as those mentioned in the seventh mode, indicating that the results are uniform across these variations.

Thirteenth Mode: Statement and Reductio Proof
Author details the thirteenth mode and its reductio proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the thirteenth mode of conditional syllogism and prove its conclusion using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Two universal conditionals yield a particular conclusion through reductio.

This lesson examines the thirteenth mode of conditional syllogism, where both premises are universal affirmative. The conclusion is a pair of particular conditionals. The author first proves it by reductio: assuming the contradictory leads to a falsehood, forcing the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
The thirteenth mode has two universal affirmative premises: 'Whenever every A is B, then J is D' (minor) and 'Whenever every B is H, then W is Z' (major). The conclusion is a pair: 'It may be that if every A is H, then J is D' and 'It may be that if every A is H, then W is Z'.
Note that the conclusion is not a single proposition but two connected particular conditionals.
2
Assume the negation of the conclusion
To use reductio, assume the contradictory of the conclusion, i.e., that it is absolutely not the case that if every A is H then J is D, or that if every A is H then W is Z. This assumption is conjoined to the true minor premise.
3
Derive a falsehood
From the conjunction of the assumed negation and the true minor premise, the author shows that a contradiction arises against the true major premise, using the third figure syllogism. This shows the assumption leads to absurdity.
The third figure syllogism is used as a tool here.
4
Conclude the original conclusion holds
Since assuming the opposite leads to a falsehood, the original conclusion must be true. This is the reductio proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثالث عشر مقدم الصغرى موجب كلي ومقدم الكبرى كذلك كما يقال كلَّما كان كل «أب» فـ (ج) د»، وكلَّما كان كل «ب هـا فــ و زا» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» ف ج د » فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «و ز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه فيضم إلى الصغرى الصادقة كما في قولنا كلما كان كل «أهـ» فكل أب وكل بهـا فكلما كان كل «أهـ» فـ و ز»، وليس البتة إذا كان قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ) فـ (ج) د » فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ فـ «وز» فإنه ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان كلما كان كل «أهـ» فكل «أب» وكل «ب هـ» فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهــه فـ اج دا ، وهذا هو الخلف لما صدق قولنا كلما كان كل «أهـ» فكل «أب» وكل ب هـا فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـه فــ ج د» وإلا لكذبت الصغرى في القياس وأما الغير فمقدم الكبرى إذا كان من لوازم مقدم الصغرى فنقول كلَّما كان كل «أب» فكل بها فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أها فـ (ج د»، وكلَّما كان كل أب فكل ب هـ) فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «وز» لينتجان من الثالث: إما الصغرى فلأنا إذا قلنا كلما كان كل «أب» فكل «أب» وكل ب هـ، وكلَّما كان كل «أب»، وكل ب هـ» فكل «أهـ» ينتج كلَّما كان كل أب» فكل بها، وإنه مع صغرى القياس ينتج من الثالث قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـا فـ «ج د». وأما الكبرى فلأنه قد يكون إذا كان كل «ب هـ» فكل «أب»، وكل «ب هـ، وكلما كان كل «أب»، وكل ب هـ» فكل «أهـ» ينتج من الثالث قد يكون إذا كان كل «أه» فـ و ز » .
The thirteenth mode: the minor premise is universal affirmative and the major premise is likewise, as when we say: 'Whenever every A is B, then J is D', and 'Whenever every B is H, then W is Z'. It produces: 'It may be that if every A is H, then J is D; it may be that if every A is H, then W is Z.' Otherwise, its contradictory would be true, so it is conjoined to the true minor, as in our saying: 'Whenever every A is H, then every A is B and every B is H, then whenever every A is H, then W and Z.' And it is absolutely not the case that if it may be that if every A is H, then J is D, then it may be that if every A is H, then W and Z—for it produces 'absolutely not if whenever every A is H, then every A is B and every B is H, then it may be that if every A is H, then J is D.' This is the reductio because our statement 'Whenever every A is H, then every A is B and every B is H, then it may be that if every A is H, then J is D' is true; otherwise the minor would be false in the syllogism. As for the other method, the major premise, if it is a consequence of the minor premise, we say: 'Whenever every A is B, then every B is H; it may be that if every A is H, then J is D', and 'Whenever every A is B, then every B is H; it may be that if every A is H, then W is Z'—they yield from the third [figure]. As for the minor, because if we say 'Whenever every A is B, then every A is B and every B is H', and 'Whenever every A is B and every B is H, then every A is H', it produces 'Whenever every A is B, then every B is H', and that with the minor of the syllogism yields from the third: 'It may be that if every A is H, then J is D.' As for the major, because it may be that if every B is H, then every A is B and every B is H, and 'Whenever every A is B and every B is H, then every A is H' yields from the third: 'It may be that if every A is H, then W and Z.'
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the thirteenth mode, where both premises are universal affirmative conditionals. The conclusion is a pair of particular affirmative conditionals: 'It may be that if every A is H, then J is D' and 'It may be that if every A is H, then W is Z.' The author first presents a proof by reductio: assuming the contradictory (i.e., that the conclusion does not hold) leads to a falsehood, which forces the conclusion. Then an alternative method is given: if the major premise is a consequence of the minor premise, a secondary syllogism in the third figure is constructed. For the minor part, the author shows how 'Whenever every A is B' entails 'Every B is H' (since from the major we have that every B is H when every A is B), and then combining with the original minor yields the desired conclusion. For the major part, similarly, the entailment from 'Every B is H' to 'Every A is B' (via the premises) is used to derive the conclusion via the third figure. This demonstrates the versatility of the syllogistic form and the use of reduction to simpler figures.

Alternative Derivation Using the Third Figure
Author provides alternative derivation using third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of the thirteenth mode by constructing a third-figure syllogism from the premises.

Core Idea
The third figure can directly produce the conclusion from the premises.

After the reductio proof, the author provides an alternative method: if the major premise is a consequence of the minor premise, we can use the third figure to derive the conclusion directly. This shows the flexibility of syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify that the major premise is a consequence of the minor
The author treats the major premise as a conditional that follows from the minor. Specifically, 'Whenever every A is B, then every B is H' (a derived statement) is used as a premise.
2
Construct the minor's third-figure syllogism
From 'Whenever every A is B, then every A is B and every B is H' (which follows from the minor), and 'Whenever every A is B and every B is H, then every A is H' (a logical truth), we get 'Whenever every A is B, then every B is H' via the third figure.
3
Combine with the original major premise
The derived 'Whenever every A is B, then every B is H' is combined with the original major 'Whenever every B is H, then W is Z' using the third figure to produce 'Whenever every A is H, then W is Z'.
4
Derive the other conclusion similarly
The same process applies to the other part of the conclusion: 'Whenever every A is H, then J is D' is derived via a parallel third-figure syllogism using the minor and a derived statement from the major.

Source Text

The 14th Mood: Definition and Structure
Definition of the 14th mood condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 14th mood of conditional syllogism by its premise combination and construct an example using placeholder variables.

Core Idea
The 14th mood is defined by a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major premise.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of the 14th mood of conditional syllogism as given by the author. The specific combination of an affirmative universal minor premise and a negative universal major premise uniquely defines this mood. Understanding this structure is essential for recognizing the mood in logical arguments and for applying the subsequent proof methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
The minor premise is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Whenever every A is B, then J D.' The major premise is a universal negative conditional: 'Whenever nothing of B is H, then W Z.'
2
Note the combination
This specific combination of affirmative minor and negative major defines the 14th mood. No other mood shares this exact pairing of quantities and qualities.
3
Understand the variables
The letters A, B, H, J, D, W, Z are placeholders for propositions. They allow the mood to be used in any subject matter without loss of generality.
In Islamic sciences, these can be replaced with real terms, e.g., from fiqh or kalam.
4
Recognize the overall structure
The two premises together are designed to yield a specific conclusion about the relation between A and H, as will be shown in the proof methods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الرابع عشر: مقدم الصغرى موجب كلي ومقدم الكبرى سالب كلي كما يقال كلما كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلَّما كان لا شيء من «ب هـ» فـ «وز» ينتج النتيجة المذكورة من قبل بالخلف وغيره. أما الخلف فهو الذي مرَّ ذكره. وأما الغير فمقدم الصغرى إذا كان من لوازم مقدم الكبرى فنقول: كلما كان كل «ب هـ» فكل «أب»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أ هـ» فـ «ج د»، وكلَّما كان كل «ب هـ» فكل «أب»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أ هـ» فـ «وز»، لينتجان من الثالث. أما الصغرى فلأنه قد يكون إذا كان كل «أب» فكل «أب» وكل «ب هـ»، وكلما كان كل «أب» وكل «ب هـ» فكل «أ هـ»، ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل «أب» فكل «أ هـ»، وإنه مع صغرى القياس ينتج من الثالث قد يكون إذا كان كل «أ هـ» فـ «ج د». وأما الكبرى فلأنه كلما كان كل «ب هـ» فكل «أب» وكل «ب هـ»، وكلما كان كل «أب» وكل «ب هـ» فكل «أ هـ»، ينتج كلَّما كان كل «ب هـ» فكل «أ هـ»، وإنه مع كبرى القياس ينتج من الثالث قد يكون إذا كان كل «أ هـ» فـ «وز».
The fourteenth mood: the minor premise is affirmative universal and the major premise is negative universal, as when it is said: 'Whenever every A is B, then J D; and whenever none of B is H, then W Z.' It yields the aforementioned result by reductio and by another method. As for reductio, it is what was mentioned earlier. As for the other method: the minor premise, if it is a concomitant of the major premise, we say: 'Whenever every B is H, then every A is B' – then it may be that whenever every A is H, then J D; and 'Whenever every B is H, then every A is B' – then it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z, so that from the third [figure] they produce: As for the minor, because it may be that whenever every A is B, then every A is B and every B is H; and whenever every A is B and every B is H, then every A is H – it yields that it may be that whenever every A is B, then every A is H; and that together with the minor of the syllogism produces from the third figure that it may be that whenever every A is H, then J D. As for the major, because whenever every B is H, then every A is B and every B is H; and whenever every A is B and every B is H, then every A is H – it yields that whenever every B is H, then every A is H; and that together with the major of the syllogism produces from the third figure that it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents the fourteenth mood of a conditional syllogism. The mood is defined by the combination of an affirmative universal minor premise and a negative universal major premise. An example is given using placeholder variables: 'Whenever every A is B, then J D' (minor) and 'Whenever no B is H, then W Z' (major). The author states that the conclusion is obtained by two methods: reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf) and another method (al-ghayr). The reductio method is referenced from earlier discussion. The other method involves transforming the premises to derive a conclusion via the third figure of syllogism. Specifically, the author shows that from the premises we can infer two conditional statements using the third figure, and then combine them to yield the desired result. The explanation is technical, detailing how the minor premise is used to derive 'it may be that whenever every A is H, then J D' and similarly for the major premise. This illustrates the logical flexibility of conditional syllogisms and the use of conversion and figure reduction.

Proof Methods for the 14th Mood: Reductio and Conversion
Two proof methods for the 14th mood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the two proof methods—reductio ad absurdum and conversion to the third figure—for the 14th mood.

Core Idea
The 14th mood can be proven either by assuming the opposite conclusion (reductio) or by transforming premises to use the third figure.

This lesson unpacks the two methods of proof mentioned by the author for the 14th mood. The first is reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf), which is standard and referenced from earlier. The second method (al-ghayr) involves converting the premises to derive intermediate conclusions via the third figure of syllogism. We will trace the steps of the second method to understand how the mood's validity is established constructively.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two premises
Minor: Whenever every A is B, then J D. Major: Whenever no B is H, then W Z.
2
Understand the reductio method
Assume the opposite of the desired conclusion. Derive a contradiction with the premises. The author says it was mentioned earlier in the text.
Reductio is a common proof technique in Islamic logic.
3
Convert the minor premise for the other method
The author states: 'the minor premise, if it is a concomitant of the major premise, we say: Whenever every B is H, then every A is B.' This transformation sets up the use of the third figure.
This step effectively swaps the subjects in the minor premise.
4
Derive intermediate conclusions via the third figure
From the transformed premises, we derive: 'Whenever every A is B and every B is H, then every A is H' (a valid conditional). Then with the original premises, we get: 'Whenever every A is H, then J D' and similarly 'Whenever every A is H, then W Z'.
The author says these are derived 'from the third figure', meaning a categorical syllogism in the third figure is used.
5
Combine the intermediate conclusions
The final result is the conjunction of the two conclusions, yielding the overall conclusion of the 14th mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The fourteenth mood: the minor premise is affirmative universal and the major premise is negative universal, as when it is said: 'Whenever every A is B, then J D; and whenever none of B is H, then W Z.' It yields the aforementioned result by reductio and by another method. As for reductio, it is what was mentioned earlier. The other method involves transforming the premises to derive a conclusion via the third figure of syllogism. Specifically, the author shows that from the premises we can infer two conditional statements using the third figure, and then combine them to yield the desired result. The explanation is technical, detailing how the minor premise is used to derive 'it may be that whenever every A is H, then J D' and similarly for the major premise. This illustrates the logical flexibility of conditional syllogisms and the use of conversion and figure reduction.

The Fifteenth Mood of Conditional Syllogism: Particular Affirmative Minor with Universal Affirmative Major
Author defines the 15th mood with particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the validity of a conditional syllogism in the fifteenth mood using conversion and the third figure.

Core Idea
Even a particular premise can combine with a universal in conditional logic to yield a valid conclusion through conversion.

The fifteenth mood is a subtle but valid form of conditional syllogism. It involves a particular affirmative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise. This lesson will guide you through the author's proof that such a combination yields a necessary conclusion, using conversion (inʿikās) and the third figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Note the minor premise is particular affirmative (e.g., 'Whenever some A is B, then J D') and the major is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Whenever every B is H, then W Z'). The final conclusion is not given directly but derived as 'it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z'.
2
Understand the conversion step
Apply conversion (ʿaks) to the minor premise: from 'some A is B' we get 'some B is A'. This transforms the first conditional into: 'Whenever J D, then some A is B' is convertible to 'Whenever J D, then some B is A'? Actually the text says: 'it may be that whenever J D, then every A is B by conversion' – check the exact wording. The conversion turns the quantifier from particular to universal? Actually, the passage states 'because it may be that whenever J D, then every A is B by conversion' – but logically, conversion of 'some A is B' gives 'some B is A', not 'every A is B'. There might be a confusion. However, the author uses a careful step that yields 'whenever J D, then every A is B' through a combination of conversion and the third figure.
The proof is technical; follow the author's steps closely.
3
Apply the third figure
Using the derived conditional and the major premise, the author applies the third figure (two premises with the same subject) to produce two intermediate conditionals. For example, from 'whenever every B is H, then W Z' and 'whenever some A is B, then J D' (after conversion) they derive 'it may be that whenever every A is H, then J D' and 'it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z'.
4
Combine to obtain the final conclusion
The two intermediate conditionals together yield the desired result: that the mood is valid, i.e., the conclusion 'it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z' follows from the premises through the chain of reasoning.

The fifteenth mood: the minor premise is affirmative particular and the major premise is affirmative universal, as when it is said: 'Whenever some A is B, then J D; and whenever every B is H, then W Z.' It yields the aforementioned result by the previously mentioned reductio and other. Or we say: 'Whenever J D, then every B is H' – then it may be that whenever every A is H, then J D; and 'Whenever J D, then every B is H' – then it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z, so that they produce from the third [figure]. As for the minor, because 'Whenever J D, then every B is H' – then it may be that whenever every A is H, then J D, because it may be that whenever J D, then every A is B by conversion, and every B is H; and whenever every A is B and every B is H, then every A is H – it yields that it may be that whenever J D, then every A is H; and it is converted to: it may be that whenever every A is H, then J D. As for the major, because then our statement is true: 'it may be that whenever J D, then every A is H'. And likewise our statement 'Whenever J D, then W Z' – they produce from the third [figure] that it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z. As for the first statement, it is as before; and as for the second, it is because the major premise together with the major of the syllogism produces it.

Commentary
This passage explains the fifteenth mood, where the minor premise is a particular affirmative and the major is a universal affirmative conditional. The other method involves converting the first premise to 'Whenever J D, then some A is B' (by conversion) and then using the third figure to derive two conditionals that together yield the desired result. The author shows step by step how the minor premise leads to 'it may be that whenever every A is H, then J D' and the major premise leads to 'it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z', thus demonstrating the validity of the mood.

Deriving Results of Remaining Conditional Moods
Author explains that from the first four moods, the results of the other three types follow by analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of any mood of a conditional syllogism given the premise types, based on the analogy with the affirmative universal moods.

Core Idea
The four affirmative moods unlock all seven conditional syllogism moods.

Having learned the four basic moods of conditional syllogisms where both premises are affirmative universal, the author now shows how the remaining three types follow by analogy. This demonstrates the systematic completeness of conditional logic, allowing you to predict conclusions without memorizing each mood separately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first four moods
Recognize that the first four moods have both premises affirmative universal (e.g., AaB, AaC?). The author calls these 'الضرب الأول' (the first set).
In conditional syllogisms, these are the basic forms from which others derive.
2
List the three remaining mood types
The remaining types are: (1) both premises negative universal, (2) minor affirmative universal + major negative universal, (3) the reverse (minor negative, major affirmative).
These cover all combinations of universal affirmative and negative premises.
3
Apply the analogy principle
Each of these three types has a conclusion that corresponds to a conclusion from the first four moods, as proven earlier. The author says the result is 'نظيرة' (corresponding).
Think of mapping a negative mood to its affirmative counterpart by flipping signs.
4
Handle the special case of both premises negative
When both premises are negative universal, both terms of the conclusion are negative. The text explicitly states: 'كل واحد من طرفي النتيجة ... سالب'.
This is the only exception mentioned; in other types, the conclusion may be affirmative.

Then if you know the results of the moods of the first of the four preliminary moods, namely those in which both premises are affirmative universal, then you know the results of the moods of the remaining three types, namely: those in which both premises are negative universal; or the minor is affirmative universal and the major is negative universal; or the opposite. For the result of each mood of these three is a corresponding result from the moods that have been mentioned by the proof that has been mentioned, except that each of the two terms of the result in the moods that are of two negative universals is negative.

Commentary
This concluding passage summarizes the relationship between the moods of conditional syllogisms. The author states that once one understands the results of the first four moods (where both premises are affirmative universal), the results of the other three types become known by analogy. These three types are: both premises negative universal; minor affirmative universal plus major negative universal; and the reverse (minor negative, major affirmative). For each of these, the conclusion is analogous to the corresponding affirmative mood, with the twist that when both premises are negative, the terms of the conclusion are negative. This indicates a systematic knowledge of all possible mood combinations.

The 16th Mode of Mixed Possibility Syllogisms
Author defines the 16th mode and proves its conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the 16th mode of mixed possibility syllogisms and reproduce its proof using conversion and third figure reasoning.

Core Idea
Mixed possibility syllogisms can be proven by converting a conditional premise to yield a categorical syllogism in the third figure.

In this lesson, we examine a complex mode where both premises are conditionals with modal qualifiers. The author demonstrates how to derive the conclusion by converting one premise and then applying a third-figure categorical syllogism. Understanding this mode will sharpen your ability to handle modal reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the minor premise: universal affirmative conditional ('Whenever every A is B, then C is D') and the major premise: universal negative conditional ('Whenever nothing from B is H, then W Z').
Both premises are possibility statements (can be read as 'it may be that...').
2
Recognize the need for conversion
The major premise is about B and H, but the conclusion involves A and H. To connect, the author converts the major premise from 'Whenever W Z, then every A is H' into 'Whenever every A is H, then W Z'.
Conversion of a conditional possibility statement is valid under specific modal conditions.
3
Use the third figure
After conversion, we have: if W Z then every A is B (from minor) and if every A is H then W Z (converted major). Using the third figure with middle term W Z, we derive: if every A is H then every A is B.
The third figure categorical syllogism requires the middle term to be subject in both premises.
4
Combine to get the final conclusion
From the derived premise (if every A is H then every A is B) and the original minor (if every A is B then C is D), we apply a hypothetical syllogism to get: if every A is H then C is D.
This matches the stated conclusion of the mode.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب السادس عشر مقدم الصغرى موجب كلي ومقدم الكبرى سالب كلي كما يقال كلما كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان لا شيء من «ب هـ» فوز ينتج النتيجة المذكورة لما مر، أو نقول كلما كان و ز فكل «أب» فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان و ز فكل «أب» فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ و ز لينتجا من الثالث. أما الصغرى فلأنه قد يكون إذا كان و ز فكل «أب» وكل ب هـ، وكلما كان كل «أب» وكل «بهـ» فكل «أهـ» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان و ز فكل «أهـ»؛ وإنه مع مقدم الصغرى ينتج كلما كان و ز فـ «ج د» فينتج مع قولنا قد يكون إذا كان و ز وكل «أه» من الثالث قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د». وأما الكبرى فلانعكاس مقدمة صادقة وهي قولنا قد يكون إذا كان و ز فكل «أهـ» إلى قولنا قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز». فالنتائج في هذه الأربعة متحدة والبراهين المستعملة فيها هي البراهين المشهورة التي مرَّ ذكرها.
The sixteenth mode: minor premise universal affirmative, major premise universal negative, as is said: Whenever every A is B, then C is D; and whenever nothing from B is H, then W Z [follows], producing the mentioned conclusion as passed. Or we say: Whenever W Z [occurs], then every A is B; it may be that whenever every A is H, then C is D; and whenever W Z [occurs], then every A is B; it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z, so that both [premises] produce from the third [figure]. As for the minor [premise], because it may be that whenever W Z [occurs], then every A is B and every B is H; and whenever every A is B and every B is H, then every A is H; it produces: it may be that whenever W Z [occurs], then every A is H. And with the minor premise it produces: whenever W Z [occurs], then C D. So it produces with our statement 'it may be that whenever W Z [occurs] and every A is H' from the third [figure]: it may be that whenever every A is H, then C D. As for the major [premise], by conversion of a true premise, our statement: 'it may be that whenever W Z [occurs], then every A is H' into our statement: 'it may be that whenever every A is H, then W Z'. So the conclusions in these four are unified and the proofs used therein are the well-known proofs that have passed.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the 16th mode of mixed syllogisms involving possibility and necessity. The mode has a universal affirmative minor ('Whenever every A is B, then C is D' — this is a conditional statement of possibility) and a universal negative major ('Whenever nothing from B is H, then W Z' — also conditional). The author shows that the conclusion is a possibility proposition. The proof uses two routes: first, by converting the major premise and using the third figure; second, by reducing to the first figure via conversion. The author notes that all four such modes share a unified proof structure.

Generalizing Syllogistic Conclusions: Polarity Rules in the First Figure
Author generalizes the conclusions of the first group of modes to three remaining groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the polarity (affirmative/negative) of each term in the conclusion of any first-figure syllogism based on the qualities of the premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion's subject mirrors the minor premise; its predicate mirrors the major premise.

We learned the four valid modes of the first figure where both premises are universal affirmative. Now the author extends this to the other three combinations of universal premises. The key insight is a simple rule: the quality (affirmative or negative) of each term in the conclusion follows the quality of the corresponding premise's term. Understanding this rule allows you to generate the conclusion without memorizing each case separately.

Guided Path
1
Recall the baseline: both premises affirmative
In the first group (both premises universal affirmative), the conclusion is a universal affirmative proposition. The subject and predicate terms are both used without negation.
This is your reference point.
2
Identify the three remaining premise-quality groups
The groups are: (1) both premises universal negative; (2) minor affirmative, major negative; (3) minor negative, major affirmative. For each, the conclusion's form is analogous to the baseline but with polarity adjustments.
3
Apply the polarity rule to derive the conclusion terms
For each group, the subject term of the conclusion takes the polarity of the minor premise's quality, and the predicate term takes the polarity of the major premise's quality. 'Polarity' here means whether the term appears as itself (affirmative) or negated (negative). For two negatives, both terms are negated; for affirmative+negative, the first term is affirmative and the second negative; for negative+affirmative, the first is negative and the second affirmative.
In other words, if the minor is affirmative, the subject term remains as given; if the minor is negative, the subject term is negated. Same for the predicate with respect to the major premise.
4
Verify with the author's subsequent examples
The author mentions he will provide an example for each group. When you encounter those examples, check that the conclusion's terms match the polarity rule described here.
This step confirms your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا عرفت نتائج ضروب الضرب الأول من الأربعة الأولى وهي ما تكون كل واحدة من المقدمتين فيها موجبة كلية فقد عرفت نتائج ضروب الأضرب الثلاثة الباقية أعنى ما تكون كل واحدة من المقدمتين سالبة كلية، أو الصغرى موجبة كلية والكبرى سالبة كلية، أو بالعكس فإن نتيجة كل ضرب من ضروب هذه الثلاثة نتيجة نظيرة من الضروب التي مرَّ ذكرها بالبرهان الذي مرَّ ذكره إلا أن كل واحد من طرفي النتيجة في ضروب ما يكون من سالبتين كليتين سالب وفيما يكون من موجبة كلية وسالبة كلية : الأول موجب والثاني سالب، وفيما يكون بالعكس على العكس، ولنذكر لكل واحد مثالاً ليقاس عليه.
Then if you know the results of the modes of the first group of the first four, which are where each of the two premises is universal affirmative, then you know the results of the modes of the three remaining groups: namely, where each of the two premises is universal negative; or the minor is universal affirmative and the major universal negative; or the opposite. For the conclusion of each mode of these three is the analogous conclusion of the modes that have passed, by the proof that has passed, except that each of the two sides of the conclusion in the modes that are from two universal negatives is negative; and in what is from a universal affirmative and a universal negative, the first [term] is affirmative and the second negative; and in the opposite case, the opposite. And let us mention an example for each to be measured against.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a generalization. After having established the conclusions for the four modes where both premises are universal affirmative (the 'first group'), the author states that the results for the three remaining groups follow analogously: (1) both premises universal negative, (2) minor affirmative major negative, (3) minor negative major affirmative. The conclusion's polarity is determined: from two negatives, both sides are negative; from affirmative+negative, the first (subject) is affirmative and the second (predicate) negative; and the opposite order gives opposite polarity. Examples will follow to illustrate.

The polarity of each term in the conclusion of a first-figure syllogism is determined directly by the quality of the corresponding premise: the subject follows the minor, the predicate follows the major.

First Mode of the Second Figure of Conditional Syllogisms
Author demonstrates the first mode of the second figure of conditional syllogisms with proof by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure and validation method of the first mode of the second figure of conditional syllogisms, and apply proof by contradiction (khalaf) to verify such syllogisms.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms have their own figures; the second figure's first mode is proven by contradiction (khalaf).

In this passage, the author presents the first mood of the second figure of conditional syllogisms. Unlike categorical syllogisms, conditional syllogisms involve premises with 'if...then' statements. To validate the conclusion, the author uses a reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) argument, assuming the opposite of the conclusion and showing it leads to a contradiction. This lesson will guide you through the structure and the proof technique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Find the two premises: (1) If every A is B, then C is D. (2) It is not at all the case that if some B are H, then W is Z. The conclusion is a complex conditional: It is not at all the case that if (if every A is H, then C is D) then (if every A is H, then W is Z).
The conclusion is nested; pay attention to the negations.
2
Understand the proof method (khalaf)
The author assumes the contradictory of the conclusion and derives a contradiction using the given premises and an additional true minor premise. This confirms the original conclusion.
Khalaf is a standard technique in Islamic logic for validating moods.
3
Follow the reduction to the second figure
The contradictory of the conclusion, combined with a true conditional linking A, H, and B, yields an impossibility via the second figure of conditional syllogisms.
This shows that assuming the opposite leads to absurdity.
4
Recognize the logical components
Note the use of strong negations (laysa al-battata) and conditional statements. The proof relies on the principle that if assuming the negation of the conclusion leads to a contradiction, the original conclusion must be true.
This is a sound logical principle used extensively in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الأول من ضروب الضرب الثاني فنقول إذا كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان بعض «ب هـا» فـ «و ز» ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان ليس البتة إذا كان كل «أ هـا» فـ «ج د» فليس البتة إذا كان كل «أ هـ» فـ «و ز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان ليس البتة إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «ج د» فليس البتة إذا كان كل «أه» فـ «و ز»؛ وإنه مع الصغرى الصادقة وهي قولنا كلما كان كل «أهـ» فكل «أب» فليس البتة إذا كان كل «أب» فـ «و ز» ينتج من الشكل الثاني ليس البتة إذا كان كلما كان كل «أ هـ» فكل «أب» فليس البتة إذا كان كل «أ هـا» فـ «ج د»، وهو خلف فإنه كلما كان كل «أ هـ» فكل «أب» فليس البتة إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د» لإنتاج مقدمها مع صغرى القياس تاليها، وإنتاج هذه المقدمة الصادقة مع الصادقة التي مرَّ ذكرها المطلوب من الثالث.
The first mode of the second figure: we say: If every A is B, then C is D, and it is not at all the case that if some B are H, then W is Z. It follows that possibly it is not the case that if it is not at all the case that if every A is H, then C is D, then it is not at all the case that if every A is H, then W is Z. Otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement: it is not at all the case that if it is not at all the case that if every A is H, then C is D, then it is not at all the case that if every A is H, then W is Z. And this [minor premise] together with the true minor premise which is our statement: whenever if every A is H, then every A is B, [then] it is not at all the case that if every A is B, then W is Z, yields from the second figure: it is not at all the case that whenever if every A is H, then every A is B, [then] it is not at all the case that if every A is H, then C is D. And this is a contradiction; for whenever if every A is H, then every A is B, [then] it is not at all the case that if every A is H, then C is D, in order that its premise with the minor premise of the syllogism produce its consequent, and the production of this true premise with the true premise previously mentioned [yields] the desired [conclusion] from the third [figure].
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage demonstrates the first mode of the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The author sets up two premises: a conditional (if every A is B, then C is D) and a negative conditional (it is not the case that if some B are H, then W is Z). The conclusion is a negative conditional involving A, H, C, D, W, Z. To prove validity, the author assumes the contradictory of the conclusion and shows that with a true minor premise (a conditional linking A, H, and B) it leads to a contradiction via the second figure. This reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) confirms the original conclusion. The argument uses the standard technique of reduction to impossibility, common in Islamic logic for validating syllogistic moods.

Modes of the Four Middle Figures of Conditional Syllogisms
Classification and rules for the four middle figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the modes of the four middle figures of conditional syllogisms and determine the conclusion's parts based on the premises' antecedents.

Core Idea
The middle figures of conditional syllogisms derive their conclusion's form from the antecedents' quality and composition.

In this passage, the author delves into the systematic classification of the four middle figures of conditional syllogisms, building on previous discussions. Understanding these modes is crucial for evaluating complex conditional arguments often used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. We'll walk through the rules that govern the quality of the conclusion based on the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure as one of the four middle figures
Recognize that the passage discusses 'الأربعة الوسطى' – a specific set of figures distinct from the first four. These figures involve conditional syllogisms where the middle term is the implication between the major premise's antecedent and one extreme of the minor premise.
2
Determine the quality of the minor premise's antecedent
Check if the antecedent of the minor premise is affirmative or negative, and universal or particular. This initial classification dictates which set of modes applies.
The minor's antecedent can be affirmative or negative, leading to different branches.
3
Apply the rule for affirmative universal antecedents
If the minor antecedent is affirmative and universal, and the major antecedent is also universal, there are four modes. The conclusion's parts mirror the premises' quality: if both affirmative, both parts are affirmative; if both negative, both are negative; if mixed, the conclusion has mixed quality.
The conclusion's antecedent and consequent correspond to the composition of the premises' antecedents.
4
Handle cases with negative antecedents
When the minor antecedent is negative, the major antecedent must also be negative. If both are universal, the conclusion has negative parts. The author provides an example using 'nothing' (لا شيء) to illustrate.
The example: 'Possibly if nothing of A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever nothing of B is H, then W is Z' yields a conclusion with 'some A are H'.
5
Use the composition (تأليف) to form the conclusion
The antecedent of the conclusion is always the result of composing (combining) the antecedents of the two premises. This composition is the key operation in these figures.
Composition may involve conjunction or other logical operations, as indicated by 'تأليف بين المقدمين'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And similarly for the remaining modes of the three remaining [figures]. As for the modes of each of the four middle [figures], the antecedent of the minor premise cannot but be affirmative or negative: if it is affirmative, then the antecedent of the major premise is universal, and it has four modes, whose results are the results of their counterparts from the previous modes by the proof previously mentioned, and they are from the third [figure]. The middle term in it is the implication of the antecedent of the major premise to one of the extremes of the minor premise. If both are negative, each of them is negative. If the minor is affirmative and the major is negative, the result has an affirmative antecedent and a negative consequent. If the antecedent of the major is particular, the result is what was mentioned due to the entailment of the universal conditional to the universality of its antecedent, even if each of the two premises is negative, as in our saying: Possibly it is not the case that if nothing of A is B, then C is D.

Commentary
This passage is a systematic classification of the modes of the four 'middle' figures of conditional syllogisms. The author explains that the antecedent of the minor premise can be affirmative or negative. If affirmative and universal, there are four modes, and the results correspond to those of the thirteenth mode of the first figure. The quality of the conclusion's parts depends on the qualities of the premises: both affirmative yield both parts affirmative; both negative yield both negative; mixed yield one affirmative and one negative. The 'middle term' is the implication linking the antecedent of the major to one extreme of the minor. If the major's antecedent is particular, the results mirror earlier modes. If the minor's antecedent is negative, the major's antecedent must also be negative, and the author gives an example with 'nothing' and derives a conclusion. The passage ends with a partial example for when both premises are negative. This comprehensive enumeration demonstrates the author's systematic approach to mapping all valid forms of conditional syllogisms.
