Conditions for Conclusion Production in Conditional Syllogisms
Author states when no conclusion follows and rules for particular and negative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a conditional syllogism yields a conclusion based on the quality and quantity of its premises.

Core Idea
Not every combination of premises produces a conclusion; negative and particular premises affect the outcome.

This passage from the logic text sets out the initial conditions for a conditional syllogism to produce a valid conclusion. The author specifies that certain combinations of premises—especially those involving particular or negative statements—either block a conclusion or result in a reversed modality. These rules are essential for evaluating conditional arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the no-conclusion case
Find the condition where the author states that no conclusion follows. The passage begins with 'وليس البتة' indicating that when 'no B is H' (a universal negative), then (W Z) does not produce a conclusion. This is a case where the premises are both negative or in a certain configuration.
The exact variables are corrupted, but the principle is clear: certain premise pairs yield no result.
2
Analyze the effect of a particular minor premise
The author says that if the minor premise (sughra) is particular (i.e., 'some A is H'), then the conclusion may have a possibility modality (قد يكون). This means that the conclusion is not necessary but possible.
A particular premise introduces uncertainty, so the conclusion is modalized.
3
Consider the case of a particular major premise
When the major premise is particular, the author calls the conclusion 'apparent' after knowing the prior rules. This implies that the same reasoning applies as in the minor premise case.
4
Apply the rule for negative minor and affirmative major
If the minor premise is negative and the major affirmative, the conclusion follows with a reversed modality. For example, from 'some A is H' the conclusion changes from 'may be' to 'may not be'.
The reversal of modality is a key point in conditional syllogisms.
5
Generalize to a fiqh example
Take a conditional syllogism: 'If the sale involves gharar (uncertainty), then it is invalid. No sale is free of gharar.' The minor is negative ('no sale is free of gharar' is equivalent to 'every sale involves gharar')? Actually, we need to match the pattern. Better: 'If the sale involves gharar, then it is invalid. Some sales involve gharar' (particular affirmative). Then the conclusion 'some invalid sales' follows with possibility.
This shows how the tool applies to real juristic reasoning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And not at all if there is no B is H then (W Z) produces 'it may be when it may not be' if some A H then (J D) so 'it may not be' if some A H then (W Z) due to what passed. And likewise if the minor premise is particular. And if the major premise is particular, then that is apparent after knowing what passed. Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions under which a conditional syllogism produces a conclusion. It begins by stating a case where no conclusion follows: when the premise 'no B is H' leads to an impossible result. The author then explains that if the minor premise is particular (i.e., 'some A is H'), a conclusion may follow with a modality of possibility (قد يكون). Similarly, if the major premise is particular, the conclusion is obvious on its own after understanding the prior rules. The passage concludes with the case where the minor premise is negative and the major affirmative, yielding a conclusion with a reversed modality. This is typical of conditional syllogisms where the modality (possibility) is transferred from the premises.

The Thirteen Moods of the Four Remaining Figures
Author enumerates moods and explains proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the thirteen moods of the four remaining conditional syllogism figures according to the quality and quantity of their premises and identify the middle term used in their proofs.

Core Idea
The thirteen moods are a systematic extension with proofs using compound middle terms and the third figure.

In earlier lessons, you learned the standard three figures of the categorical syllogism. The present passage extends this analysis to conditional syllogisms, introducing four additional figures with thirteen moods each. The author not only lists them but provides a proof structure using a compound middle term called the 'result of composition.' This lesson will guide you through the classification and proof method of these moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the total number of moods
The passage states that for each of the last four figures, there are thirteen moods. Find this explicit statement in the Arabic.
The phrase is 'فثلاثة عشر'.
2
Categorize the moods into two main groups
The author divides the thirteen moods: eight have the major premise negative; four have the minor premise affirmative and the major premise universal; one has both premises particular affirmative. Locate these categories in the text.
The division is based on the quality and quantity of the premises.
3
Understand the 'result of composition'
The term 'نتيجة التأليف' (natijat al-ta'lif) is an intermediate proposition opposite in quality to the major premise. It is not the final conclusion but a relational statement used in the proof.
The passage warns: 'لا النتيجة اللازمة مع المقدمتين'.
4
Identify the middle term in proofs
For the eight moods with negative major, the middle term is the conjunction (ملازمة) of the result of composition with the major premise. For the four moods with affirmative minor and universal major, it is the conjunction of the minor premise with one part of the major premise.
The middle term is always a compound proposition, not a single term.
5
Trace an example proof
The passage gives an example: from 'no A is B' and 'every B is H', one derives a conclusion about 'some A is H'. Follow how the middle term 'every B is H and no A is H' is used to entail 'no A is B' and then the conclusion.
The proof relies on the third figure and the compound middle term.

البراهين ما يبرهن به إنتاجه. وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من «أب» فــ «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «ب هـا فـ اوز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل أه» فـ (ج) د ) فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «اوز» لأنه كلَّما د
As for the moods of each of the last four figures, they are thirteen: eight of them have the premise negative and their conclusions are the conclusions of their counterparts from the preceding moods; the proof for them is from the third figure, and the middle term is the conjunction of the result of composition with the major premise. And it is necessary that the result of composition be opposite in quality to the major premise so that together with the major premise it yields the minor premise. The meaning of 'result of composition' is the relation of the larger term to the smaller in an opposite way to the major premise, not the necessary conclusion from the two premises, because obtaining a conclusion from a negative minor in the first figure is impossible. Example: Whenever 'no A is B' then (J D), and it may be that 'every B is H' then (W Z) produces: it may be that 'not some A is H' then (J D), and it may be that 'not some A is H' then (W Z). The middle term is: whenever 'every B is H' then 'no A is H' because then it may be that 'no A is H' and 'every B is H', and whenever that is the case then 'no A is B'. And likewise it may be that 'no A is H' then (W Z). If the major premise is particular, it also yields this result due to what passed, but the middle term in it is: whenever 'some B is H' then 'no A is H'. If the minor premise is particular, it is apparent. And likewise if the major premise is one of the two negatives. And upon this if both premises are negative or only one is negative, each of them is known by knowing what passed. And four [moods] have the minor premise affirmative and the major premise universal. Their conclusions are conclusions of their counterparts from the preceding moods, and the middle term in their proofs is the conjunction of the minor premise with one of the two parts of the major premise, as we say: Whenever 'every A is B' then (J D), and it may be that 'every B is H' then (W Z) produces: it may be that 'some A is H' then (J D), and it may be that 'some A is H' then (W Z) due to what passed. If the minor premise is particular, it is apparent. And if the major premise is universal negative, as we say: Whenever 'every A is B' then (J D), and it may be that 'no B is H' then (W Z) produces: it may be that 'some A is not H' then (J D), and it may be that 'some A is not H' then (W Z) due to what passed. Likewise if the minor premise is particular. And upon this in the remaining moods. And as for another mood among them: what has the minor premise affirmative particular and the major premise likewise. The middle term is the conjunction of the converse of the result of composition with the major premise, as we say: Whenever 'some A is B' then (J D), and it may be that 'some B is H' then (W Z) produces: it may be that 'some A is H' then (J D), and it may be that 'some A is H' then (W Z). And upon this in the remaining moods. And it has passed in the first mood of the proofs what proves each mood, known by reflection. The clearest of the proofs arranged from the third figure is what has the middle term as the conjunction of the two minor and major premises for what is common between the two parts of the conclusion, as we say: 'every A is H' or 'no A is H' for example. Then for each of the two premises, if it is affirmative and one of them is universal, there is discussion: whether it must produce in all sixteen moods in every figure of every division? And there is consideration, for among the proofs are those that prove its production. That is because if our statement 'it may be that if no A is B then (J D)' is true, and 'whenever every B is H then (W Z)' produces 'it may be that every A is H then (J D)' and 'it may be that every A is H then (W Z)' because whenever...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the moods of the conditional syllogisms in the last four figures (likely referring to figures beyond the first three in some extended classification). The author states that there are thirteen moods for each of these four figures. Eight of these moods have the major premise negative, and their conclusions are analogous to conclusions of earlier moods (from previous figures). The proof for these moods is drawn from the third figure. The key mechanism is that the middle term is the conjunction ('ملازمة') of the 'result of composition' (نتيجة التأليف) with the major premise. The 'result of composition' here is a relational proposition linking the larger term to the smaller in a way opposite to the major premise, not the final conclusion itself. The author then gives an example with variables A, B, H, J, D, W, Z. For instance, from 'no A is B' and 'every B is H', one can derive a modal conclusion about 'some A is H' with the possibility operator. The middle term in this proof is the proposition 'every B is H' conjoined with 'no A is H', which together entail 'no A is B'. This shows that the middle term links the premises. The passage continues to discuss special cases: if the major premise is particular, or if the minor is particular, the same result follows but with a different middle term. It then addresses the remaining five moods (the four mentioned plus one more?) which have the minor premise affirmative and the major universal. The proof for these uses the middle term as the conjunction of the minor premise with one part of the major premise. An example is given: from 'every A is B' and 'every B is H', we get 'some A is H'. The author also considers the case where the major premise is universal negative, leading to a negative conclusion. Finally, he touches on a mood where both premises are particular affirmative, and the middle term involves the converse of the result of composition. The passage ends with a reflective note on whether all sixteen moods in every figure necessarily produce a conclusion, suggesting some debate and that certain proofs may be defective. This is a sophisticated analysis of conditional syllogisms, likely from a later work in the Islamic logical tradition that expands on the standard treatment of the first three figures.

Use variables like 'A', 'B', 'H' for theological terms.

The Syllogism

Understanding the Third Figure Conditional Syllogism
Author explains the basic structure of third figure conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main types of third figure conditional syllogisms and explain the role of the middle term as an implication linking antecedents.

Core Idea
In third figure conditionals, the middle term is a logical implication that joins the antecedents.

In this passage, the author builds on previous discussions of conditional syllogisms by focusing on the third figure. He describes two primary forms: one where the minor premise's antecedent is affirmative and the major's universal, and another where both particular. The crucial element is the 'middle term' (al-awsat) which acts as an implication (mulazama) connecting the antecedents. Understanding this structure is key before delving into the more complex controversy over the 16 moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Find the two cases the author describes: first case (minor antecedent affirmative, major universal) and second case (both particular). The text labels them clearly.
First type: minor antecedent affirmative, major antecedent universal. Second type: both particular and affirmative.
2
Extract the middle term
Notice that the middle term is not a single term but an implication: it links the antecedent of the minor to one extreme of the major, or the converse of the composition's result to the major's antecedent.
The author says: 'والأوسط في براهينها ملازمة مقدم الصغرى أحد طرفي الكبرى'.
3
Trace the example
Take the first example: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D' and 'it may be that if some A is H, then W is Z'. See how the conclusion combines: 'it may be that if some A is H, then C is D' and 'it may be that if some A is H, then W is Z'. The middle term (implication) makes this possible.
Variables: A, B, C, D, H, W, Z.
4
Note the generalization
The author says 'and so on for the rest of the moods' indicating these patterns generalize.
The pattern is intended to be applicable to all similar moods.

منها ما يكون مقدم الصغرى فيه موجبًا ومقدم الكبرى كليا. ونتائجها نتائج نظائرها من الضروب المقدمة، والأوسط في براهينها ملازمة مقدم الصغرى أحد طرفي الكبرى كقولنا: كلما كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان بعض «أه» فـ «وز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان بعض «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، فقد يكون إذا كان بعض «أهـ» فـ «وز» لما مر. وإن كان مقدم الصغرى جزئيا فظاهر. وأما إذا كان مقدم الكبرى سالبا كليا كقولنا: كلما كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من «ب هـ» فـ «وز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان بعض «أ» ليس هـ» فـ «ج د»، فقد يكون إذا كان بعض «أ» ليس هـ» فـ «وز» لما مر. وكذلك إذا كان مقدم الصغرى جزئيا. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب.

وأما الضرب الآخر منها فذلك ما يكون مقدم الصغرى فيه موجبا جزئيا؛ ومقدم الكبرى كذلك. والأوسط ملازمة عكس نتيجة التأليف لمقدم الكبرى كقولنا: كلما كان بعض «أب» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان بعض «ب هـ» فـ «وز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان بعض «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، فقد يكون إذا كان بعض «أهـ» فـ «وز». وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب؛ وقد مر في الضرب الأول من البراهين ما يبرهن به كل ضرب يعرف بالتأمل.

والأظهر من البراهين المنتظمة من الشكل الثالث ما يكون الأوسط ملازمة مجموع مقدمي الصغرى والكبرى للمشترك بين طرفي النتيجة كقولنا: كل «أهـ» أو لا شيء من «أهـ» مثلا. ثم كل واحدة من المقدمتين إذا كانت موجبة وإحديهما كلية ففيه من الكلام أنه هل يجب إنتاجه في جميع الضروب الستة عشر في كل شكل من كل قسم: وفيه نظر فإن من البراهين ما يبرهن به إنتاجه.

وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا: قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «ب هـ» فـ «وز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز» لأنه كلما كان كل «أهـ» فكل «ب هـ»، ولا شيء من «أب» فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «أهـ» فكل «ب هـ»، ولا شيء من «أب» فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز». وكذلك إذا كان كلما كان لا شيء من «أب» وكل «ب هـ» فكل «أهـ» لأنه حينئذ قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز» أيضا.

ولما ظهر إنتاجه من الشكل الثالث في هذا القسم بهذين الطرفين فقد ظهر إنتاجه بهما في سائر الأقسام. وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى كلية فقط: فإن منهم من زعم أنه لا ينتج وإنه ينتج بهذين الطرفين في قولنا: كلما كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان بعض «ب هـ» فـ «وز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز». ومن زعم أنه لا ينتج فعليه أن يبين؛ إذ البيان غير مسطور في الكتب المشهورة كما ينبغي.
Among them is that whose minor premise's antecedent is affirmative and the major premise's antecedent is universal. Their conclusions are conclusions of their counterparts from the prior moods, and the middle term in their proofs is the implication of the minor premise's antecedent to one of the two extremes of the major premise, as we say: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D,' and it may be that if some A is H, then W is Z, yielding: it may be that if some A is H, then C is D, and it may be that if some A is H, then W is Z, as previously. And if the minor premise's antecedent is particular, it is clear. As for if the major premise's antecedent is universally negative, as we say: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D,' and it may be that if no B is H, then W is Z, yielding: it may be that if some A is not H, then C is D, and it may be that if some A is not H, then W is Z, as previously. Likewise if the minor premise's antecedent is particular. And so on for the rest of the moods.

As for the other type from them, that is that whose minor premise's antecedent is affirmative particular, and the major premise's antecedent similarly. The middle term is the implication of the converse of the composition's result to the major premise's antecedent, as we say: 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D,' and it may be that if some B is H, then W is Z, yielding: it may be that if some A is H, then C is D, and it may be that if some A is H, then W is Z. And so on for the rest of the moods; and it has passed in the first mood of the proofs that each mood is proved by reflection.

And the most apparent of the proofs arranged from the third figure is that whose middle term is the implication of the conjunction of the antecedents of the minor and major premises to what is common between the two extremes of the conclusion, as we say: 'Every A is H' or 'nothing of A is H' for example. Then each one of the two premises if it is affirmative and one of them universal, then there is discussion about whether its production is necessary in all sixteen moods in every figure from every division: and there is consideration, for among the proofs there is that by which its production is proved.

That is because if our statement is true: 'It may be that if nothing of A is B, then C is D,' and 'Whenever every B is H, then W is Z,' yields: 'It may be that if every A is H, then C is D' and 'It may be that if every A is H, then W is Z' because whenever every A is H, then every B is H, and nothing of A is B, so it may be that if every A is H, then C is D, and whenever every A is H, then every B is H, and nothing of A is B, so it may be that if every A is H, then W is Z, so it may be that if every A is H, then C is D, and it may be that if every A is H, then W is Z. And likewise if it is that whenever nothing of A is B and every B is H, then every A is H, because then it may be that if every A is H, then C is D, and it may be that if every A is H, then W is Z also.

And when its production from the third figure in this division appeared by these two ends, its production by them in the rest of the divisions appeared. Likewise if the minor premise is universal only: for among them is he who claims that it does not produce and that it does produce by these two ends in our saying: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D,' and 'It may be that if some B is H, then W is Z,' yields: 'It may be that if every A is H, then C is D' and 'It may be that if every A is H, then W is Z.' And he who claims that it does not produce, he must demonstrate; for the demonstration is not recorded in the well-known books as it ought to be.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion of valid moods in the third figure of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author begins by describing two types: one where the minor premise's antecedent is affirmative and the major's is universal, and another where both are particular. For each, he gives example arguments using variables (A, B, C, D, H, W, Z) showing how a conclusion with two consequents (C-D and W-Z) is derived. The middle term's role is to link the antecedents. Notably, the most evident proofs are those where the middle term is the implication of the conjunction of both antecedents to a common term in the conclusion. The author then addresses a controversy: whether all 16 possible combinations in the third figure are valid. He provides two specific proof patterns that demonstrate validity, asserting that these suffice for all divisions. Finally, he notes a dispute regarding when the minor premise is universal only, and states that the proof is not recorded in standard books, leaving it as an open problem.

The Controversy over the 16 Moods
Author addresses controversy over validity of all 16 moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the controversy about the validity of all 16 moods in the third figure conditional syllogism and describe the two proof patterns that the author presents as sufficient.

Core Idea
Only two proof patterns are needed to establish the validity of all 16 moods in the third figure.

The author moves from describing basic types to a more advanced discussion: must all 16 possible combinations of premises (with different quantities and qualities) in the third figure yield valid conclusions? He presents two specific arguments (using the ends as he calls them) that demonstrate validity, and claims these suffice for all divisions. There is, however, disagreement among logicians, and the author invites those who deny it to produce a counterproof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed claim
The claim is: in the third figure, if each premise is affirmative and one is universal, does the syllogism produce a conclusion in all 16 moods?
The 16 moods refer to possible combinations of quantity and quality of the two premises.
2
Examine the first proof
The author gives an example: 'It may be that if nothing of A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever every B is H, then W is Z' yields conclusions. The proof uses two intermediates: the conjunction of the antecedents leads to a common term.
The proof shows that if the premises are granted, then 'if every A is H, then C is D' and 'if every A is H, then W is Z' follow.
3
Examine the second proof
The second pattern uses a different configuration: 'Whenever nothing of A is B and every B is H, then every A is H' leads to the same conclusions.
This is a variation that also yields the result.
4
Understand sufficiency
The author claims that if these two ends work, they work for all divisions. This is a meta-argument about coverage.
The phrase 'بهذين الطرفين' means 'by these two ends'.
5
Note the open problem
The author ends by mentioning that when the minor premise is universal only, some claim it doesn't produce, and the proof is not recorded in books, leaving room for further investigation.
The author challenges the denier to provide a demonstration.

والأظهر من البراهين المنتظمة من الشكل الثالث ما يكون الأوسط ملازمة مجموع مقدمي الصغرى والكبرى للمشترك بين طرفي النتيجة كقولنا: كل «أهـ» أو لا شيء من «أهـ» مثلا. ثم كل واحدة من المقدمتين إذا كانت موجبة وإحديهما كلية ففيه من الكلام أنه هل يجب إنتاجه في جميع الضروب الستة عشر في كل شكل من كل قسم: وفيه نظر فإن من البراهين ما يبرهن به إنتاجه.

وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا: قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «ب هـ» فـ «وز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز» لأنه كلما كان كل «أهـ» فكل «ب هـ»، ولا شيء من «أب» فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان كل «أهـ» فكل «ب هـ»، ولا شيء من «أب» فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز». وكذلك إذا كان كلما كان لا شيء من «أب» وكل «ب هـ» فكل «أهـ» لأنه حينئذ قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز» أيضا.

ولما ظهر إنتاجه من الشكل الثالث في هذا القسم بهذين الطرفين فقد ظهر إنتاجه بهما في سائر الأقسام. وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى كلية فقط: فإن منهم من زعم أنه لا ينتج وإنه ينتج بهذين الطرفين في قولنا: كلما كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان بعض «ب هـ» فـ «وز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «ج د»، فقد يكون إذا كان كل «أهـ» فـ «وز». ومن زعم أنه لا ينتج فعليه أن يبين؛ إذ البيان غير مسطور في الكتب المشهورة كما ينبغي.
Among them is that whose minor premise's antecedent is affirmative and the major premise's antecedent is universal. Their conclusions are conclusions of their counterparts from the prior moods, and the middle term in their proofs is the implication of the minor premise's antecedent to one of the two extremes of the major premise, as we say: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D,' and it may be that if some A is H, then W is Z, yielding: it may be that if some A is H, then C is D, and it may be that if some A is H, then W is Z, as previously. And if the minor premise's antecedent is particular, it is clear. As for if the major premise's antecedent is universally negative, as we say: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D,' and it may be that if no B is H, then W is Z, yielding: it may be that if some A is not H, then C is D, and it may be that if some A is not H, then W is Z, as previously. Likewise if the minor premise's antecedent is particular. And so on for the rest of the moods.

As for the other type from them, that is that whose minor premise's antecedent is affirmative particular, and the major premise's antecedent similarly. The middle term is the implication of the converse of the composition's result to the major premise's antecedent, as we say: 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D,' and it may be that if some B is H, then W is Z, yielding: it may be that if some A is H, then C is D, and it may be that if some A is H, then W is Z. And so on for the rest of the moods; and it has passed in the first mood of the proofs that each mood is proved by reflection.

And the most apparent of the proofs arranged from the third figure is that whose middle term is the implication of the conjunction of the antecedents of the minor and major premises to what is common between the two extremes of the conclusion, as we say: 'Every A is H' or 'nothing of A is H' for example. Then each one of the two premises if it is affirmative and one of them universal, then there is discussion about whether its production is necessary in all sixteen moods in every figure from every division: and there is consideration, for among the proofs there is that by which its production is proved.

That is because if our statement is true: 'It may be that if nothing of A is B, then C is D,' and 'Whenever every B is H, then W is Z,' yields: 'It may be that if every A is H, then C is D' and 'It may be that if every A is H, then W is Z' because whenever every A is H, then every B is H, and nothing of A is B, so it may be that if every A is H, then C is D, and whenever every A is H, then every B is H, and nothing of A is B, so it may be that if every A is H, then W is Z, so it may be that if every A is H, then C is D, and it may be that if every A is H, then W is Z. And likewise if it is that whenever nothing of A is B and every B is H, then every A is H, because then it may be that if every A is H, then C is D, and it may be that if every A is H, then W is Z also.

And when its production from the third figure in this division appeared by these two ends, its production by them in the rest of the divisions appeared. Likewise if the minor premise is universal only: for among them is he who claims that it does not produce and that it does produce by these two ends in our saying: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D,' and 'It may be that if some B is H, then W is Z,' yields: 'It may be that if every A is H, then C is D' and 'It may be that if every A is H, then W is Z.' And he who claims that it does not produce, he must demonstrate; for the demonstration is not recorded in the well-known books as it ought to be.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion of valid moods in the third figure of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author begins by describing two types: one where the minor premise's antecedent is affirmative and the major's is universal, and another where both are particular. For each, he gives example arguments using variables (A, B, C, D, H, W, Z) showing how a conclusion with two consequents (C-D and W-Z) is derived. The middle term's role is to link the antecedents. Notably, the most evident proofs are those where the middle term is the implication of the conjunction of both antecedents to a common term in the conclusion. The author then addresses a controversy: whether all 16 possible combinations in the third figure are valid. He provides two specific proof patterns that demonstrate validity, asserting that these suffice for all divisions. Finally, he notes a dispute regarding when the minor premise is universal only, and states that the proof is not recorded in standard books, leaving it as an open problem.

ملازمة

Counting Valid Moods in the Second Figure
Author enumerates total valid moods in second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the total of 176 valid moods in the second figure is derived from the author's grouping of premises.

Core Idea
The second figure has 176 valid moods, sum of groups: 64 from first four, 64 from middle four, 48 from last four conditioned by quality.

The author presents a systematic count of valid moods. You will learn to parse the enumeration and verify the arithmetic. This enumeration helps in quickly assessing the validity of syllogistic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups of moods
The author mentions 'first four', 'middle four', and 'last four' moods. Each group contains four moods, probably based on the pattern of universal and particular quantification of the antecedents and consequents in the premises.
The exact classification is not fully specified in this passage, but the grouping is evident from the text.
2
Determine each group's contribution
The first four each produce 16 moods across all their subtypes. The middle four also produce 16 each. The last four produce only 12 each because of restrictions.
Total: 4*16 + 4*16 + 4*12 = 64+64+48 = 176.
3
Understand the restrictions for the last four
Out of the 12 moods for each of the last four, 8 occur when the antecedents of the two premises agree in quality (both affirmative or both negative), and 4 occur when they differ and the major premise's antecedent is universal.
These conditions are necessary for validity in the second figure.
4
Verify the arithmetic
Add the contributions: 64 (first four) + 64 (middle four) + 48 (last four) = 176. This matches the author's statement.
If the last four had no restrictions, each would produce 16, giving a total of 192.

As for the second figure, the productive according to the conditions are one hundred and seventy-six, by necessity of the production of each one of the first four in all their moods; that is sixteen. This passage deals with the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The author asserts that the total number of productive (valid) moods in the second figure, according to the given conditions, is 176. Constructing a Valid Conditional Syllogism in the Second Figure
Author illustrates a demonstration with variables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the demonstration of a valid second figure conditional syllogism using the author's variables.

Core Idea
A second figure conditional syllogism chains two conditionals to produce a new conditional through an assumed antecedent.

The author provides a concrete example with variables to show how a conclusion follows. Although the text is cut off, we can follow the logical structure. This will help you understand the production of conclusions in the second figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two given premises
The author gives: Premise 1: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D.' Premise 2: 'Whenever every H is B, then W is Z.' Note the variable 'اوز' likely stands for WZ.
The Arabic uses letters ألف, باء, جيم, دال, هاء, واو, زاي. For clarity, we use A, B, C, D, H, W, Z.
2
Understand the desired conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional: 'If whenever every A is H, then C is D, then whenever every A is H, then W is Z.' In other words, if the antecedent 'every A is H' implies C-D, then it also implies W-Z.
The conclusion is itself a conditional linking two conditionals.
3
Assume the antecedent of the conclusion
To prove the conclusion, assume 'whenever every A is H'. The author then derives two intermediate conditionals: first, 'whenever every A is H, then C is D' and second, 'whenever every A is H, then W is Z'.
The derivation steps are: from the assumption and the premises, we can deduce that every A is H leads to C-D via the first premise and a chain, and similarly to W-Z.
4
Follow the logical chain
The author says: 'because whenever every A is H, then every H is B' - this step links the assumption to the second premise. Then from 'every H is B' and premise 2, we get 'whenever every H is B, then W is Z', so under the assumption, we get W-Z. Similarly, from 'every A is H' we can also derive C-D via premise 1? The text is cut off, but the pattern is clear: the assumption triggers both premises.
This chain shows that the second figure syllogism produces a compound conditional conclusion.

Enumerating Productive Moods in the Second Figure of Conditional Syllogisms
Author enumerates the productive moods of the second figure with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of productive moods in the second figure of conditional syllogisms based on the conditions given in the text.

Core Idea
The 176 productive moods arise from combinatorial conditions on antecedents.

In this passage, the author explains how many productive moods exist in the second figure of conditional syllogisms. Understanding these counts requires careful attention to the groupings of premises and the conditions under which they produce valid conclusions. This lesson guides you through the enumeration and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of premises
The author divides the premises into three sets: the first four, the middle four, and the last four. These likely correspond to different types of conditional propositions based on quality and quantity of antecedents.
The exact nature of these groups is not detailed, but the counting depends on them.
2
Count moods from the first and middle categories
Each of the first four premises yields 16 moods, and each of the middle four also yields 16 moods. So total from these two categories is 4×16 + 4×16 = 128.
3
Count moods from the last category with conditions
The last four premises yield moods only under two conditions: 8 moods when the antecedents of the two premises agree in quality (both affirmative or both negative), and 4 moods when they differ and the antecedent of the major is universal. Each of the last four yields 8+4=12 moods, total 4×12=48.
The conditions are specified in the text: agreement in quality, or difference with major universal.
4
Sum to find the total productive moods
Add the totals: 128 (from first and middle) + 48 (from last) = 176 productive moods for the second figure.
This matches the author's number: مائة وستة وسبعون.
5
Examine an example derivation
The author provides an example: from premises 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever every E is B, then W is Z', a conclusion involving A and E is derived via the third figure. This illustrates how the moods are actually produced.
The derivation uses symbolic variables (A, B, E, C, D, W, Z) as placeholders for terms.

الصغرى كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان كل «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلَّما كان لا شيء من «هب» ف و ز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من «أهـ) فــ اج دا فقد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من «أهــا فــ و ز»، والبيان من الثالث قد مر من قبل غير مرة على الخصوص في الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول. وكذلك إذا كان مقدم الكبرى سالبًا جزئياً؛ فإن كان مقدم الصغرى موجبا ينتج النتيجة المذكورة لما مر وإن كان موافقا لمقدم الكبرى في الكيف كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان كل «أب»
As for the second figure, the productive [moods] according to the conditions are one hundred and seventy-six, necessarily the production of each of the first four in all of its moods; that is sixteen. And the production of each of the middle four in all of its moods likewise; that is also sixteen. And the production of each of the last four; that is in twelve of the moods, namely every mood in which the antecedents of the two premises agree in quality: that is eight moods. And every mood in which the antecedents of the two premises differ along with the antecedent in the major being universal: that is four moods. As for the explanation of each mood of the first four, that is like what passed in the first figure when our statement is true: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D', and 'Whenever every E is B, then W is Z' produces 'It may be that whenever every A is C (?) then every A is W (?)' because 'Whenever every A is E' and 'Whenever every A is E, then C is D', and 'Whenever every A is E, then every E is B' and 'Whenever every A is E, then W is Z', and they both are produced from the third; as for the minor, because then 'Whenever every A is E, then every A is B' and it together with the minor produces 'Whenever every A is E, then C is D'. As for the major, due to the production of its antecedent with the major of the syllogism even if the two premises are negative as in our statement: 'Whenever nothing of A is B, then C is D', and 'Whenever nothing of E is B, then W is Z' produces 'It may be that it is not absolutely the case that whenever every A is E, then C is D, then it is not absolutely the case that whenever every A is E, then W is Z' because 'Whenever every A is E, then every E is B' and 'it is not absolutely the case that whenever every A is E, then C is D', and 'Whenever every A is E, then every E is B' and 'it is not absolutely the case that whenever every A is E, then W is Z', and the explanation is as before. And if the minor is affirmative and the major negative, it produces: 'It may be that whenever every A is E, then C is D, then it is not absolutely the case that whenever every A is E, then W is Z.' And if the reverse, it produces: 'It may be that it is not absolutely the case that whenever every A is E, then C is D, then whenever every A is E, then W is Z.' And the middle is what passed from the conjunction, but from the consequences it is necessary that the first premise in the first mood be affirmative of the consequent and the second negative, and in the second mood the reverse. And likewise for the remaining moods of these four. As for the moods of the middle four, their explanation is from the third figure; for if the antecedent of the minor differs from the antecedent of the major in quality, then the middle in it is the conjunction of the antecedent of the major to the antecedent of the minor, as in our statement: 'It may be that if every A is B, then C is D', and 'Whenever nothing of E is B, then W is Z' produces 'It may be that if nothing of A is E, then C is D, then it may be that if nothing of A is E, then W is Z', and the explanation from the third has already passed many times, especially in the first mood of the first figure. And likewise if the antecedent of the major is particular negative; then if the antecedent of the minor is affirmative, it produces the mentioned result as before, and if it agrees with the antecedent of the major in quality as in our statement: 'It may be that if every A is B, then C is D'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage discusses the productive moods of the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The author states that according to the conditions, the second figure yields 176 productive moods. This number arises from the production of each of the first four groups of premises in all their moods (16), the middle four groups also in all their moods (another 16), and the last four groups in twelve moods: eight moods where the antecedents of the two premises agree in quality, and four moods where the antecedents differ with the antecedent in the major being universal. The author then explains the first four moods by analogy with the first figure. For example, if we have two conditionals: 'Whenever every A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever every E is B, then W is Z', the conclusion is 'It may be that whenever every A is E, then C is D then every A is W (?)' or similar. The explanation involves showing that from the minor and major premises, we can derive a new conditional via the third figure. The author also considers cases where premises are negative, affirmative, or mixed, showing how the conclusion changes. The middle four moods are derived from the third figure, specifically when the antecedent of the minor differs in quality from that of the major. The explanation is said to have been given earlier, especially in the first mood of the first figure. The passage uses symbolic variables (A, B, E, C, D, W, Z) to represent terms in conditional propositions, following the standard practice of Aristotelian logic as adapted in Islamic logic.

Total Productive Moods of the Third Figure
Author enumerates productive moods of third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the total number of productive moods in the third figure of conditional syllogism and explain its three categories.

Core Idea
The third figure yields 176 productive moods, but only the first four and last four are fully productive; the middle four are limited.

In conditional syllogisms, the third figure has a well-defined set of valid moods. This lesson unpacks the total count of 176, showing how they are divided into three groups: the first four, the last four, and the middle four. Understanding this classification is essential for mastering which argument forms in the third figure are valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the total number
The passage states that the third figure has one hundred and seventy-six productive moods. Memorize this number as the first fact.
2
Break into three categories
The author divides these into three groups: the first four, the last four, and the middle four. The first four and last four are each productive in all their variants (i.e., every combination of quality and quantity), while the middle four are only productive under a specific condition.
The exact meaning of 'first four' and 'last four' is likely based on the quality of the major premise (affirmative/negative) but requires clarification from the full text.
3
Understand the condition for the middle four
The middle four moods are productive only when the minor premise matches the major premise in quality (both affirmative or both negative). This condition is analogous to what was earlier presented for the first figure.
The passage says 'meaning those where the minor premise is affirmative or negative matching the major premise in quality.'
4
Apply the breakdown to a diagram
Draw a simple diagram: Total = 176. Categories: First four (fully productive), Last four (fully productive), Middle four (productive in 12 specific moods). The 12 comes from 4 moods × 3 quality combinations? This detail is implied but not fully explicit.
The passage says 'and the middle four in twelve moods,' so each of the middle four moods produces only three specific valid forms.

Condition for Productive Middle Four Moods
Author specifies condition for middle four moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the middle four moods in the third figure are productive and apply the condition of matching quality.

Core Idea
The middle four moods only work when both premises agree in quality — both affirmative or both negative.

Not all moods in the third figure are equally productive. The middle four have a specific restriction: the minor premise must match the major premise in quality. This lesson focuses on that condition and how it reduces the number of valid forms from sixteen to twelve.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three categories
From the previous lesson, remember that the first four and last four are fully productive, while the middle four are limited to 12 moods.
2
State the condition explicitly
The condition for the middle four is that the minor premise must have the same quality as the major premise: both affirmative or both negative. The passage says: 'أعني ما يكون مقدم الصغرى موجباً أو سالبا موافقا لمقدم الكبرى في الكيف' (meaning those where the minor premise is affirmative or negative matching the major premise in quality).
Quality refers to affirmative or negative, not quantity.
3
Understand why this matters
If the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), the middle four moods do not produce a valid conclusion. This reduces the number of valid forms from 4×4=16 to 12 (4 moods × 3 quality combinations? Actually, for each of the 4 middle moods, there are 4 possible quality combinations: AA, AN, NA, NN. The condition allows only two: AA and NN. But the passage says 'twelve moods,' so perhaps each mood has three valid forms? This detail may require deeper study, but the key is the condition of matching quality.
The exact derivation of 12 is not fully explained in this excerpt; it likely involves additional restrictions from quantity.
4
Apply the condition to an example
Consider a middle mood: if (every B is A) then (C is D) and if (every B is H) then (W is Z). For this to be productive, both conditionals must share the same quality (both affirmative or both negative). If one is affirmative and the other negative, the mood is not productive.
The example given in the passage is for a first mood, not a middle one, but the principle applies.

Deriving Third Figure Moods via Implication
Author enumerates moods of third figure and explains derivation of the first mood of each group

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a third-figure conditional syllogism to its equivalent first-figure form by identifying the middle term's implication.

Core Idea
Third figure moods are reduced to the first figure by finding the appropriate implication (mulāzamah) between premises.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has 176 productive moods, but they are not all primitive. The author shows how to derive each mood by using the logical relation of implication (ملازمة) to transform the middle term's position. This lesson focuses on the first mood of each group, demonstrating how the middle term's role changes depending on whether the mood belongs to the first four, middle four, or last four. Understanding this derivation is essential for mastering modal syllogistic as applied in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood group
Note whether the mood belongs to the first four, middle four, or last four of the third figure. This determines how the middle term (الأوسط) relates to the premises: in first four, the minor premise implies one extreme; in middle four, one extreme of the major implies the minor; in last four, one extreme of the minor implies the major.
The groups are based on the quality and modality of the minor premise relative to the major.
2
Write the conditional premises
Represent each premise as a conditional: 'whenever (every B is A) then (J is D)' for the minor, and 'whenever (every B is H) then (W is Z)' for the major. The middle term B appears in the antecedent of both conditionals.
The Arabic text uses 'كلما كان كل ب أ فـ ج د' for universal-affirmative conditionals.
3
Find the implication (ملازمة)
For the first mood of the first four, the middle is 'ملازمة مقدم الصغرى لأحد طرفي الكبرى' – the minor premise's antecedent (every B is A) implies one of the two extremes of the major premise's consequent (e.g., J or D). Use this to transform the syllogism into a first-figure form.
The implication is a logical necessity (mulāzamah) that allows substitution of terms.
4
Reduce to the first figure
By applying the implication, you can rewrite the premises so that the middle term becomes the subject of the major and predicate of the minor, as in the first figure. Then the conclusion follows directly, and the mood is validated.
This reduction relies on the rules of modal conversion and the principle of transitivity.
5
Repeat for other groups
For the middle four, the implication is from one extreme of the major to the minor premise; for the last four, from one extreme of the minor to the major. Practice each pattern to internalize the structural differences.
The total 176 moods arise from combinations of these patterns with different quantifiers and modalities.

And as for the third figure, its productive moods are one hundred and seventy-six, producing each one of the first four and the last four in all their moods; and the middle four in twelve moods, meaning that the minor premise is affirmative or negative in agreement with the major premise in quality and the explanation is as passed in the first figure. As for the first mood of the first four: it is 'whenever every B is A, then J is D', and 'whenever every B is H, then W is Z' yields 'it may be that if some A is H, then J is D, and it may be that if some A is H, then W is Z' as before. The middle is the implication of the minor premise for one of the two extremes of the major premise or vice versa, until the proof of this mood occurs according to the mentioned ways in the first figure. And as for the exposition of the first mood of the middle four: it is 'it may be that if every B is A, then J is D', and 'whenever every B is H, then W is Z' yields the mentioned result; and the middle therein is the implication of one of the two extremes of the major premise for the minor premise. And as for the first mood of the last four: it is 'whenever every B is A, then J is D', and 'it may be that if every B is H, then W is Z' yields the mentioned result, and the middle is the implication of one of the two extremes of the minor premise for the major premise, for example.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification and derivation of the moods of the third figure in modal syllogistic. The author states that the third figure has 176 productive moods, which are divided into three groups: the first four moods, the middle four, and the last four. The total is derived from the combination of premise types. Then he explains the first mood of each group using symbolic terms (A, B, J, D, H, W, Z) and conditional statements ('whenever... then...' and 'it may be that if... then...'). The key is the 'middle term' (الأوسط) which is always the subject of both premises (B in each 'every B is...' or 'some B is...'). The derivation relies on converting these moods to the first figure by using the implication (ملازمة) between premises. For the first mood of the first four, the middle is the implication of the minor premise for one of the extremes (terms) of the major premise, meaning a logical transformation that brings it to the first figure. Similarly for the other groups. This shows how the third figure's moods are analyzed in terms of the first figure.

Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Enumerates the valid moods of the fourth figure and provides examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the productive moods of the fourth figure in conditional syllogisms and determine their validity based on the antecedent conditions.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields 168 valid conditional syllogisms, grouped by the minor premise's antecedent type.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates the moods of the fourth figure for conditional syllogisms. He explains that each of the first four antecedent types produces moods in all their variations, while the middle and last groups produce in thirteen moods each, with specific restrictions on the major premise. This enumeration helps the logician recognize valid argument forms and avoid invalid ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Recognize that in the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor and the subject of the major (or vice versa). Here, the middle term is the accompaniment (mulazama) of the minor's antecedent to one of the major's extremes.
This is a conditional syllogism, so the 'terms' are propositions, not categorical terms.
2
Understand the grouping
The 168 moods are grouped into three categories: first four, middle four, and last four, based on the antecedent of the minor premise (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative).
The first four produce moods in all their variations; the middle and last each produce 13 moods.
3
Apply conditions
For each group, note the restrictions. For example, the middle group's 13 moods require that the major's antecedent is not universal affirmative. The last group's 13 moods require that the minor's antecedent is particular negative.
These conditions ensure the validity of the syllogism.
4
Verify with example
Take the example from the first four: 'Whenever every B is A, then C is D' and 'Whenever every H is B, then W is Z'. The middle term is B, the accompaniment between the antecedents. Check that the conclusion follows logically.
The author says 'as before' – refer to earlier lessons if needed.

As for the fourth figure, the productive from it is one hundred and sixty-eight, because each of the first four (groups) produces in all its moods; and the middle (group) in thirteen moods: the universal affirmative antecedent from the minor with the four, and the universal negative and the particular affirmative with the three – meaning if the major premise's antecedent is not universal affirmative – and the particular negative with the two negatives. And from the middle four: It may be if every B is A, then C is D; and whenever every H is B, then W is Z, the mentioned result follows, as before. And from the last four: Whenever every B is A, then C is D; and if every H is B, then W is Z, the mentioned result also follows, as before; and all is known by reflection after examining what preceded.

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the productive moods of the fourth figure in conditional syllogisms. The author breaks down the total of 168 valid moods into groups: the first four, the middle four, and the last four. The first four produce moods in all their variations, while the middle four produce in thirteen moods each, and the last four also produce in thirteen moods each. The conditions depend on the quantity and quality of the antecedent of the minor premise. Examples are given using variable letters: from the first four, when every B is A implies C is D, and every H is B implies W is Z, the conclusion follows with the middle term being the accompaniment of the minor's antecedent to the major's extreme. Similarly for the other groups. The author concludes that all this is known through reflection after studying what came before.

Shared Middle Term in Compound Syllogisms
Defines the second division with shared middle term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second division of non-perfect syllogisms (shared middle term) and determine its productive moods based on the given conditions.

Core Idea
A shared middle term between two consequents creates compound syllogisms requiring careful alignment of accompaniment (mulazama).

In the previous lesson, we covered the first division of non-perfect syllogisms. Now we turn to the second division, where the middle term is common to the two consequents of conditional premises. This structure underpins many arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. We will explore the conditions for productivity and the three resulting moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional premises
Look for two statements of the form 'Whenever X, then Y' and 'Whenever P, then Q'. The shared middle term is the term that appears in both consequents (Y and Q) or connects them via accompaniment.
The passage uses variables (ج د, أب, وز, به) for illustration.
2
Apply the first condition
Check that the two consequents, when combined as a syllogism, are productive (yield a valid conclusion) and that both premises are affirmative with at least one universal. This condition yields three moods: two universal affirmatives, one particular affirmative with one universal affirmative, or the inverse of a universal affirmative.
The inverse here likely refers to converting a universal affirmative to a particular affirmative.
3
Determine the specific mood from the three
Classify the pair of premises according to the three types: (1) both universal affirmative, (2) one particular affirmative and one universal affirmative, (3) the inverse of a universal affirmative. Each mood produces a conclusion in every figure with its own specific moods.
The author states the proof comes from the third figure and the middle term (mulazama) accompanies the antecedent of the universal to one extreme of the other.
4
Analyze the logical notation
The passage uses variables: e.g., 'ج د' (C D), 'أب' (A B), 'وز' (W Z), 'به' (B H). The conditional 'كلما كان ج د فـ أب' means 'Whenever C is D, then A is B'. The argument chains these conditionals to produce a conclusion.
The details of the chaining are complex; the key point is the middle term (here the accompaniment relation) connects the extremes.

And the second division of this division [i.e., the second division, which is the non-perfect]: it is that in which the middle term is shared between the two consequents. Commentary
This passage defines the second division of non-perfect syllogisms, where the middle term is common to the two consequents (i.e., the conditional propositions share a consequent or antecedent? Actually 'between the two consequents' means the middle term appears in both consequents). There are two conditions: first, the consequents together with the premises form a productive syllogism with affirmative premises and at least one universal. Second, either the composition's conclusion plus the consequent of one premise (agreeing in quality) yields the other consequent, or the composition's conclusion with the universal affirmative consequent yields the negative consequent. From the first condition, three moods arise: two universal affirmatives, particular affirmative + universal affirmative, and the inverse. Each figure has its own specific moods under these. The proof is from the third figure? The author then explains the first mood of the first figure with an example: whenever C is D then A is B, and whenever W is Z then every B is H, then it may be that whenever C is D then every A is H, etc. The reasoning involves chaining conditionals. The middle term in the first mood is the accompaniment of the antecedent of the universal to an extreme of the other. For the second mood, the middle term accompanies the major's antecedent to a minor's extreme, and the third mood reverses this. The text ends mid-sentence, but the logical structure is clear.

The First Condition of Conditional Syllogisms and Its Three Moods
Enumerates three productive moods under the first condition of conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three productive moods under the first condition of conditional syllogisms and explain the role of the middle term.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms with shared consequents produce valid conclusions when both premises are affirmative and at least one is universal.

In this passage, the author moves beyond simple conditional syllogisms to a more complex type where the two premises share a common consequent as the middle term. Understanding the first condition and its three moods is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic theological and juristic reasoning. We will unpack the condition, enumerate the moods, and see how the proof works using logical variables.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional syllogism
Notice that the middle term (al-awsat) is shared between the two consequents (tāliyān). This is the second division of 'imperfect' conditional syllogisms. The goal is to produce a conclusion from two conditional premises.
2
Understand the first condition
The first condition for productivity requires that both premises be affirmative (ījāb al-muqaddimatayn) and at least one of them be universal (kullīyat iḥdāhumā). Without this, no conclusion follows in this type.
It does not require both to be universal; one universal suffices.
3
Enumerate the three moods
Under this condition, there are exactly three moods: (1) both premises universal affirmative, (2) one universal affirmative and one particular affirmative, (3) the converse (i.e., the converse of one of the previous combinations). The author states: 'mina l-mūjibatayn al-kulliyatayn, aw min al-mūjiba al-juziyya ma‘a al-mūjiba al-kulliyya, aw min al-‘aks'.
The third mood is derived by conversion (al-‘aks) of one premise.
4
Examine the role of the middle term
The middle term is the implication (mulāzama) of the antecedent of the universal premise to one of the extremes of the other premise. This ensures that the conclusion follows. For the first mood of the first figure, the author gives a proof using variables (ج, د, أ, ب, و, ز).
Don't get lost in the variables; focus on the structural role of implication.

And the second division of this division [i.e., of the second division, which is the imperfect]: it is that in which the middle term is shared between the two consequents, and the condition concerning it is of two types. One of them is the inclusion of the two consequents in a productive composition with the affirmation of both premises and the universality of one of them. The second is either producing the result of the composition with the consequent of one of the two premises agreeing in quality with the consequent of the other, or producing the result of the composition with the consequent of the universal affirmative to the consequent of the negative. So the productive moods according to the first condition are three, namely from two universal affirmatives, or from a particular affirmative with a universal affirmative, or from the converse. And that which is produced in each of the three moods in each figure [has] its particular moods of that figure, and the proof from the third, and the middle term in it is the implication of the antecedent of the universal to one of the extremes of the other, so that the proof of the first mood occurs on the mentioned faces, the proof of the second mood on the first two faces, and the proof of the third mood on the remaining two faces. Commentary
This passage defines the second division of conditional syllogisms and explains the first condition for productivity. The author begins by stating that the middle term in these syllogisms is the common consequent shared between two conditionals. The condition for productivity is divided into two types: the first requires that the two consequents form a productive composition when both premises are affirmative and at least one is universal. Under this first condition, three moods are enumerated: from two universal affirmatives, from a particular affirmative with a universal affirmative, or from the converse (i.e., conversion). The author then explains how each mood yields a proof, focusing on the role of the middle term as the implication of the antecedent of the universal premise to one of the extremes of the other. He gives a detailed example for the first mood of the first figure, using variables (ج, د, أ, ب, و, ز) to illustrate the logical structure. The example shows a chain of conditionals leading to a conclusion. This is a highly technical exposition typical of advanced treatments of conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic.

The Twelve Moods of Conditional Syllogisms under the Second Condition
Enumeration of twelve productive moods under second condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any pair of conditional premises by their quality (affirmative/negative) and identify which of the twelve productive moods they belong to.

Core Idea
Twelve moods arise from the interplay of quality in conditional premises.

The second condition for productive conditional syllogisms yields exactly twelve moods, organized by whether the premises agree or differ in quality (affirmative or negative). Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating the validity of complex conditional arguments used in kalam and fiqh. This lesson unpacks the eight agreeing-quality moods and the four differing-quality moods, providing a systematic method to recognize them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality of each premise
Determine whether each conditional premise is affirmative (e.g., 'if A then B') or negative (e.g., 'if A then not B'). Mark them as both affirmative, both negative, or one of each.
Quality here refers to the whole conditional, not the parts.
2
Classify as agreeing or differing in quality
If both premises are affirmative or both negative, they agree in quality (المتفقتان في الكيف). If one is affirmative and the other negative, they differ (المختلفتان في الكيف).
3
Count the mood from agreeing quality
From agreeing quality there are eight moods. Four come from two affirmative premises; the other four from two negative premises. The author gives examples with variables to show the conclusion forms.
The conclusion is always a particular conditional (جزئية متصلة).
4
Count the mood from differing quality
From differing quality there are four moods. Examples include a universal affirmative minor with a universal negative major, and variations.
The conclusion may differ based on the order and quantity of the premises.
5
Match the mood to the pattern
Using the given examples, practice mapping actual conditional premises (e.g., from fiqh) to these patterns. For instance, 'If it is wine, then it is haram' (affirmative) and 'If it is not wine, then it is halal' (negative) differ in quality.
Apply to real Islamic arguments.

وأما الضروب المنتجة بحسب الشرط الثاني فهي اثنا عشر ضربًا ثمانية: من المتفقتين في الكيف، وأربعة من المختلفتين. والأربعة من الثمانية ما يكون من موجبتين، والنتيجة فيها متصلة جزئية مقدماها سالبان جزئيان كقولنا قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د) فكل (أب)، وقد يكون إذا كان (وز) فبعض (أب هـا) ينتج قد يكون إذا كان ليس كلما كان (ج) د) فكل (أه) فليس كلما كان (وز) فكل (أهـ). ولو كان التالي من الكبرى منتجا لتالي الصغرى كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د) فلا شيء من (أب)، وقد يكون إذا كان (وز) فكل (أب هـا) ينتج قد يكون إذا كان قد لا يكون إذا كان (د) فلا شيء من (أهـا) فقد لا يكون إذا كان (وزا) فلا شيء من (أهـا). وأما الأربعة الأخرى من المقدمتين فالنتيجة فيها هي النتيجة المذكورة كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان (أج د) فكل (أب)، وقد لا يكون إذا كان (وز) فبعض (ب هـا) ينتج قد يكون إذا كان ليس كلما كان (ج) د) فكل (أهـ) فليس كلما كان كل (وز) فكل (أه)، هذا إذا كان تالي الصغرى مع نتيجة التأليف منتجا لتالي الكبرى. أما إذا كان بالعكس كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان (ج) د) فلا شيء من (أب)، وقد لا يكون إذا كان (وز) فكل (أب هـا) ينتج قد يكون إذا كان ليس كلما كان (ج) د) فلا شيء من (أهـ) فليس كلَّما كان (وز) فلا شيء من (أهـ). وأما الأربعة الحاصلة من المختلفتين في الكيف كالصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية وبالعكس في الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى السالبة الجزئية، وبالعكس.
And as for the productive moods according to the second condition, they are twelve moods: eight from those agreeing in quality, and four from those differing in quality. The four from the eight are [those] from two affirmatives, and the result in them is a particular conditional, its two antecedents are two particular negatives, like our saying: 'It may be that if (j) d) then every (a b), and it may be that if (w z) then some (a b h a)' produces 'it may be that if not whenever (j) d) then every (a h), then not whenever (w z) then every (a h)'. And if the consequent of the major produces the consequent of the minor, as in our saying: 'It may be that if (j) d) then nothing of (a b), and it may be that if (w z) then every (a b h a)' produces 'it may be that if it may not be that if (d) then nothing of (a h a), then it may not be that if (w z) then nothing of (a h a)'. As for the other four from the two premises, the result in them is the mentioned result, as in our saying: 'It may not be that if (a j d) then every (a b), and it may not be that if (w z) then some (b h a)' produces 'it may be that if not whenever (j) d) then every (a h), then not whenever every (w z) then every (a h)'; this is when the consequent of the minor with the result of the composition produces the consequent of the major. As if the converse, as in our saying: 'It may not be that if (j) d) then nothing of (a b), and it may not be that if (w z) then every (a b h a)' produces 'it may be that if not whenever (j) d) then nothing of (a h), then not whenever (w z) then nothing of (a h)'. And as for the four obtained from the differing in quality, like the universal affirmative minor with the universal negative major, and the converse in the universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major, and the converse.

Commentary
This passage turns to the second condition for productivity in conditional syllogisms. Under this condition, twelve moods are enumerated: eight from premises that agree in quality (both affirmative or both negative) and four from premises that differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). The author then details the four from agreeing quality that are affirmative, giving an example with variables and showing how the conclusion is a particular conditional with particular negative antecedents. He also discusses the case when the consequent of the major produces the consequent of the minor, and vice versa. Then he covers the four from differing quality, illustrating with examples of universal affirmative and universal negative combinations. This completes the enumeration of the twelve moods.

The Twelve Productive Moods of the Second Condition
Author enumerates and exemplifies the 12 productive mood combinations in the second condition of conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the 16 mood combinations under the second condition of conditional syllogisms into productive and non-productive categories, identifying the 12 valid moods.

Core Idea
Only 12 out of 16 premise combinations yield a valid conclusion in conditional syllogisms under the second condition.

This passage enumerates the twelve productive moods of the second condition in conditional syllogisms. The author shows that from the sixteen possible combinations of premises (based on quality and quantity), only twelve produce a valid conclusion. Understanding this classification is crucial for constructing valid conditional arguments in Islamic logic, especially in kalam where conditional reasoning is frequent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories: agreement and difference in quality
The author divides productive moods into eight from premises agreeing in quality (both affirmative or both negative) and four from premises differing in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Recognize that this classification is based on the quality of the premises.
2
Examine the four affirmative-affirmative moods (from agreeing quality)
The author gives an example: two affirmative premises with particular conclusions. Note the pattern: 'If (J is D) then every A is B' and 'If (W is Z) then some A is B' produce 'If not every time (J is D) then every A is H, so not every time (W is Z) then every A is H.'
The conclusion is a particular conditional with negative antecedent and consequent.
3
Examine the four negative-negative moods (from agreeing quality)
The other four from agreeing quality are both negative premises. The conclusion follows a similar form but with negation. For example, two 'may not be' premises produce a conclusion with 'may be' and negation.
The author states the conclusion is 'the mentioned conclusion', meaning the pattern is analogous.
4
Examine the four moods from differing quality
These involve one affirmative and one negative premise. The author gives examples such as universal affirmative minor with universal negative major, and also the reverse. The conclusion's antecedent matches the minor's quality and consequent matches the major's quality.
Pay attention to which premise is affirmative and which is negative.
5
Identify the four non-productive moods
The author notes that the remaining four of the sixteen are not productive. These are the combinations that do not appear in the productive list. Understand that not all quality-quantity combinations yield a valid conclusion under this condition.
The passage also mentions the property of producing two particular premises, but not absolutely.

As for the productive moods according to the second condition, they are twelve moods: eight from agreement in quality, and four from difference. The four from the eight are those from two affirmative premises, and the conclusion is a particular conditional whose antecedent is two particular negatives, as in our saying: 'It may be that if (J is D) then every A is B, and it may be that if (W is Z) then some A is B' yields 'It may be that if not every time (J is D) then every A is H, so not every time (W is Z) then every A is H.' And if the consequent of the major is productive of the consequent of the minor, as in our saying: 'It may be that if (J is D) then no A is B, and it may be that if (W is Z) then every A is B' yields 'It may be that if it may not be that if (J is D) then no A is H, so it may not be that if (W is Z) then no A is H.' As for the other four from the two premises, the conclusion is the mentioned conclusion as in our saying: 'It may not be that if (J is D) then every A is B, and it may not be that if (W is Z) then some A is B' yields 'It may be that if not every time (J is D) then every A is H, so not every time (W is Z) then every A is H'—this is when the consequent of the minor along with the result of composition produces the consequent of the major. As for the reverse, as in our saying: 'It may not be that if (J is D) then no A is B, and it may not be that if (W is Z) then every A is B' yields 'It may be that if not every time (J is D) then no A is H, so not every time (W is Z) then no A is H.' As for the four obtained from differing in quality, such as the universal affirmative minor with the universal negative major, and vice versa in the universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major, and vice versa, the conclusion is the mentioned conclusion when the consequent of the affirmative along with the result of composition produces the consequent of the negative. Its antecedent is like the minor in quality, and its consequent like the major, as in our saying: 'Every time (J is D) then every A is B, and it may not be that if (W is Z) then some A is B' yields 'It may be that if (J is D) then every A is H, so it may not be that if (W is Z) then every A is H.' And if reversed, as in our saying: 'It may not be that if (J is D) then no A is B, and every time (W is Z) then every A is B' yields 'It may be that if it may not be that if (J is D) then no A is H, so it may be that if (W is Z) then no A is H.' As for the remaining four of the sixteen moods, they are not productive; and among the properties of this section is the production of the two particular premises as previously mentioned in the agreement in quality, not absolutely.

Commentary
